### 2NC - ! -- Ocean Acidification

#### Decarbonization is key to stop ocean acidification -- we are on the brink of total ecosystem collapse

Lisa **Bachelor**, 6/9/**25**, The Guardian, "Ticking timebomb: sea
acidity has reached critical levels, threatening entire ecosystems -
study,"
https://www.theguardian.com/environment/2025/jun/09/sea-acidity-ecosystems-ocean-acidification-planetary-health-scientists?utm_source=chatgpt.com,
mm

[The world's [oceans]{.mark}]{.underline} are in worse health than
realised, scientists have said today, as they warn that a key
measurement shows we [[are "running out of time]{.underline}]{.mark}" to
protect marine ecosystems. [Ocean [acidification]{.mark}]{.underline},
often called the "evil twin" of the climate crisis, [[is caused when
carbon dioxide is rapidly absorbed]{.mark} by the ocean]{.underline},
where it reacts with water molecules leading to a fall in the pH level
of the seawater. It damages coral reefs and other ocean habitats and, in
extreme cases, can dissolve the shells of marine creatures. [Until now,
ocean acidification had not been deemed to have crossed its "planetary
boundary]{.underline}". The planetary boundaries are the natural limits
of key global systems -- such as climate, water and wildlife diversity
-- beyond which their ability to maintain a healthy planet is in danger
of failing. Six of the nine had been crossed already, scientists said
last year. [However, [a new study]{.mark}]{.underline} by the UK's
Plymouth Marine Laboratory (PML), the Washington-based National Oceanic
and Atmospheric Administration and Oregon State University's
Co-operative Institute for Marine Resources Studies [[found that]{.mark}
ocean acidification's "boundary" was also reached]{.underline} about
five years ago. ["[Ocean acidification]{.mark}]{.underline} isn't just
an environmental crisis -- it **[[is a ticking
timebomb]{.underline}]{.mark}** [for marine ecosystems and coastal
economies]{.underline}," said PML's Prof Steve Widdicombe, who is also
co-chair of the Global Ocean Acidification Observing Network. The study
drew on new and historical physical and chemical measurements from ice
cores, combined with advanced computer models and studies of marine
life, which gave the scientists an overall assessment of the past 150
years. [It found that [by 2020 the average ocean condition worldwide
was]{.mark} already [very close to]{.mark}]{.underline} -- and in some
regions beyond -- [[the planetary boundary]{.mark} for ocean
acidification]{.underline}. This is defined as when the concentration of
calcium carbonate in seawater is more than 20% below preindustrial
levels. The deeper in the ocean they looked, the worse the findings
were, the scientists said. At 200 metres below the surface, 60% of
global waters had breached the "safe" limit for acidification. "Most
ocean life doesn't just live at the surface," said PML's Prof Helen
Findlay. "The waters below are home to many more different types of
plants and animals. Since these deeper waters are changing so much, [the
impacts of ocean acidification could be far worse than we
thought]{.underline}." This had, she added, huge implications for
important underwater ecosystems such as tropical and even deep-sea coral
reefs that provided essential habitats and nursery grounds for the young
of many species. As pH levels drop, calcifying species such as corals,
oysters, mussels and tiny molluscs known as sea butterflies struggle to
maintain their protective structures, leading to weaker shells, slower
growth, reduced reproduction and decreased survival rates. The authors
underlined that **[[decreasing CO2 emissions was the only way to deal
with acidification globally]{.underline}]{.mark}**, but that
conservation measures could and should focus on the regions and species
that were most vulnerable. Jessie Turner, director of the International
Alliance to Combat Ocean Acidification, who was not involved in the
study, said: "This report makes it clear: we are running out of time and
what we do -- or fail to do -- now is already determining our future.
"**[[We are coming to terms with an existential
threat]{.underline}]{.mark}** while grappling with the difficult reality
that much suitable habitat for key species has already been lost. It's
clear that governments can no longer afford to overlook acidification in
mainstream policy agendas," she said.

#### Ocean collapse is a [unique threat]{.underline} to human survival through [oxygen]{.underline}, [food]{.underline}, and [climate]{.underline}. 

**Stone '16** \[Gregory; November 9; PhD in Marine Science from the
University of the South Pacific, Executive Vice President and Chief
Scientist for Oceans at Conservation International; World Economic
Forum, "We Cannot Survive Without Our Oceans. We Must Act to Save Them
-- Now,"
https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2016/11/an-ocean-renaissance-is-key-to-our-survival/\]

The oceans of the Earth can exist without us. If we humans become
extinct, they won't even notice we're gone. [[We]{.mark}, however,
**simply [cannot exist]{.mark}** [without]{.mark} our [oceans]{.mark}.
We need them. [They are the]{.mark} **primary [life support
system]{.mark}** [on Earth]{.mark}, the **lungs**, **climate
regulator**, and ultimate **food factory**]{.underline} - connected
deeply to each of us and nearly every economic activity that makes our
world go round. For those old enough to remember - and for the young and
curious, too - you may know "Earthrise," the iconic photograph taken on
24 December, 1968 by a 35-year-old American astronaut and engineer named
William Anders as he looked through a small, frosty window of Apollo 8,
the first spacecraft to leave Earth's gravity and orbit the moon. From
380,000km away, our spectacular planet appeared as a vulnerable, mostly
blue sphere, vividly illustrating their size and importance -- they
cover some 70% of the Earth with an average depth of four kilometers.
Since then, we've explored just 10% of our oceans' contents. We've
learnt they provide 99% of the Earth's living space and contain around
80% of all living organisms. They are the largest space in our universe
known to be inhabited by organisms. [[They provide]{.underline}]{.mark}
our [Earth with a moist, livable climate, full of
[oxygen]{.mark}]{.underline} from trillions of microscopic plants
floating like tiny stars in a watery, immense universe, producing close
to 100 million tons of food each year, enough to provide one in every
four or five people with their daily protein. [They are]{.underline}
also [[crucial to]{.mark} our **[climate]{.mark} system**]{.underline}.
There is more heat stored in the first 10 feet of the ocean than in the
entire atmosphere. This is significant because heat is transferred from
the atmosphere to the ocean. Then currents move heat throughout the
ocean and across the planet. The gulf stream, an intense warm current,
is the major source of north-south heat transfer in the Atlantic. It
starts in the Gulf of Mexico, snakes around Florida, and then goes
northeast across the Atlantic, expanding like a funnel until it gets to
Europe. The heat transferred by this "river within an ocean" brings a
mild, moist, warm climate to Europeans. The gulf stream moves this heat
at a speed 300 times the average speed of the Amazon, and at a volume
greater than all rivers in the world, combined. Without the gulf stream,
England would be like Alaska and upper Norway would resemble Antarctica.
But for all its might, the gulf stream is fragile and could change, or
even shut down within 10 years if we keep heating our planet the way we
are, disrupting the exquisite balance of this unusual oceanographic
phenomena. By any measure, scientific or anecdotal, the global ocean
makes Earth a nice place for us all to live. [If you want to see what
the Earth might be like without an ocean there are plenty of examples in
our own solar system]{.underline}. President John F. Kennedy once said:
"Why it is that all of us are so committed to the sea? It's because we
all came from there \... and when we go back \... we are going back from
whence we came." He was also correct in his prediction that the United
States would land people on the moon within 10 years, and that the 1960s
would be the "Decade of the Oceans." Sadly, in spite of the birth of
modern oceanography, our oceans did not garner anywhere near the same
budget and attention as space exploration. We looked up instead of
taking note of the trouble brewing right beneath our noses, along our
shores and under our docks. Since the 1970s, a lot of damage was done
when countries all over the world developed their coastal areas,
destroying reefs, salt marshes, and mangroves along the way. Our
population grew from four to over seven billion people, and as the
middle class expanded so did consumption, especially in the realm of
oil, coal and natural gas development. Newly minted fleets of fishing
boats depleting most of our wild stocks of edible ocean life. We
polluted our oceans with agricultural runoff, oil, plastic and other
synthetic chemicals. The time is now Through the thousands of dives I
conducted during this period in all five oceans, I had a front row seat
to this seemingly endless destruction. Fortunately, this is changing.
Countries all over the world now understand that the blue planet, as
Anders saw it from the Apollo spaceship nearly 50 years ago, is an ocean
planet, and it's on us to make sure we never forget that. So don't.
Three landmark agreements in 2015 give us the international framework we
need for action. The Paris climate summit acknowledged that if we are to
understand and solve climate change, oceans must be part of the
equation. There was renewed evidence of the governance of the 'High
Seas' -- the area beyond any national jurisdiction. And the UN also
adopted 17 Sustainable Development Goals. Goal 14 focuses on conserving
our oceans. This makes 2015 the most important year in the history of
the world as far as committing to take collective action on the global
ocean. It should be clear to anyone concerned with the health of our
civilization that our past connection to the ocean is ripe and ready to
connect with a future vision; one that is fully awake to the reality
that if we are to continue occupying this planet in balance with nature
we must pay attention to the oceans - now.
[[Conserving]{.underline}]{.mark} our [[oceans -- and **through
them**]{.mark} **our [own survival]{.mark}** [-- is]{.mark} now more
[possible]{.mark} than ever]{.underline}. It will need to include all
stakeholders -- governments, businesses, citizens, technology
developers, and indigenous populations. We are all stakeholders of the
oceans and we all have something to contribute. Indigenous communities
have a deep knowledge of oceans based on empirical information as old as
humanity itself. For example, Aborigines in present-day Australia living
alongside the ocean discovered what time of year particular coral reef
fish are most nutritious and not reproducing, which becomes the best
time of year to harvest and eat them. This is a time-tested, perfect
system, which marine biologists and nutritionists knew nothing about
until recently. We also need the innovation that well-funded, modern
science can provide. We are in the midst of what some call the \"Fourth
Industrial Revolution\", one that embraces big data, apps, and unbridled
creativity. Initiatives like Global Fishing Watch are increasing
transparency and giving data access to ordinary people -- on their
mobile phones and in real time! I am encouraged by the optimistic
millennials who intend to change the world through approaches so new we
have no idea what they are. Our oceans need a renaissance This is our
moment. It's what I call the "Ocean Renaissance" -- a moment where
technology meets tradition, where fishermen become scientists, where
scientists become policy makers, where policy makers become
anthropologists and where anthropologists learn the ways of the oceans
from cultures like the Aborigines or Pacific Islanders, who live a
symbiotic life with the ocean. The conversation will continue at the UN
climate conference in Morocco this month. Even if you cannot attend, you
can watch a livestream feed on the U.N. website, you can read about what
happens there, and you can do something with your newfound education.
Our collective actions now impact the health of the ocean. The effect of
global industry, commerce and the lifestyle choices of more than seven
billion people is affecting our oceans. We have a choice. We can degrade
the salty water that covers most of our planet or we can steward it in a
way that enables it to take care of us. [[Without healthy
oceans]{.underline}]{.mark}, our [[life on Earth would
be]{.underline}]{.mark} severely challenged, unpleasant and perhaps
[**[impossible]{.mark}**. The [oceans are]{.mark} the **[life
support]{.mark} system** of all living beings]{.underline}. That's
because **[[life on Earth]{.underline}]{.mark}** can thrive without
land, but it [[**cannot** exist without an ocean]{.underline}]{.mark}.

### UQ -- Decarbonization Now

#### The U.S. is successfully decarbonizing its economy now -- growth has become decoupled from GHG emissions

Roger **Pielke**, 6/16/**25**, American Enterprise Institute, "The Most
Amazing Climate Policy Figure,"
https://www.aei.org/articles/the-most-amazing-climate-policy-figure/, mm

[You'd be hard pressed to find a more fascinating straight
line.]{.underline} The figure below is one of the most amazing graphs in
all of climate policy.1 It shows the decarbonization of the U.S. economy
from 1992 to 2025 --- with decarbonization is defined as the ratio of
carbon dioxide emissions (from fossil fuels) to GDP (in 2025\$).
![](media/image6.png){width="3.810721784776903in"
height="3.0876246719160103in"} I was motivated to update this time
series after reading the best analysis of recent U.S. climate policy
that I've seen, by Ted Nordhaus of The Breakthrough Institute. Ted's
entire, detailed analysis is well worth your time, but the part that
sent me off making graphs was this: The wild swings of energy policies
from Democratic to Republican administrations beg the question of
whether the United States is capable of sustaining any coherent energy
policy, whether in service of climate mitigation or energy dominance.
And yet, [despite]{.underline} all of that political and [policy
volatility, the US over recent decades has both managed to sustain a
significant and consistent rate of decarbonization while establishing
its energy economy]{.underline} as the envy of much of the world. [What
makes the U.S. decarbonization rate remarkable is]{.underline} that it
is (a) not an explicit focus as a policy metric, (b) few people pay
attention to it (you'll see decarbonization discussed more here at THB
than most anywhere), and, (c) U.S. politics and energy policy has indeed
been subject to "wild swings" over the past 30+ years. And yet, with all
that, [the linear decline]{.underline}. Consider another remarkable
graph. The figure below shows, for 1992 to 2025, a scatterplot of U.S.
GDP versus U.S. carbon dioxide emissions from fossil fuels.
![](media/image7.png){width="6.320298556430446in"
height="2.6732042869641295in"} The graph shows that from 1992 to 2005,
increasing GDP was fairly tightly coupled to increasing emissions of
carbon dioxide. Then, in [about 2006]{.underline}, things changed ---
since then, [GDP has been decoupled from emissions of carbon
dioxide]{.underline} --- [GDP has continued to increase as emissions
fall.]{.underline} Decarbonization results from the net effects of
decreasing carbon intensity of energy consumption and/or decreasing
energy intensity of the economy. We can make some sense of causality
with the figure below, which shows from 2006 the change in U.S.
consumption of coal, natural gas, and renewables.3
![](media/image8.png){width="3.1214862204724407in"
height="2.1605839895013124in"} The figure shows that [from 2006 to 2024,
coal consumption decreased rapidly]{.underline}, and by slightly less
than the combined increase in natural gas and renewables. [Natural gas
consumption increased]{.underline} dramatically due largely to
technologies such as fracking and horizontal drilling, along with
learning through implementation by the private sector. Because natural
gas has about half the carbon intensity of coal, and renewables much
less, [these]{.underline} consumption [trends contributed to emissions
going down]{.underline} even as economic activity increased by about 25%
(after inflation). How long can decarbonization continue based on
replacing coal with less carbon intensive generation? The figure below
gives us a sense of the answer.
![](media/image9.png){width="4.200164041994751in"
height="2.532846675415573in"} The figure shows that the decline of coal
has been just about linear since about 2006. [If that trend were to
continue, then the U.S. would be coal-free by the early 2030s,
suggesting that the linear rate of U.S. decarbonization could continue
for the rest of the decade without a significant departure from recent
energy trends]{.underline}. However, at some point the decline of coal
will stop --- either because the U.S. has exited coal or the decreasing
trend in coal consumption of the past decade is stopped or reversed. At
that point, if the decades-long systematic decarbonization of the U.S.
economy is to continue, then some other dynamic will have to kick in.
Some concluding thoughts: We do not fully understand the linear rate of
decarbonization over the past three decades --- What dynamics have
modulated this trend such that it has been so consistent? If we
understood that trend better, we'd be better positioned to understand
what it would take to keep it going; If we kept that decarbonization
trend going, the U.S. would hit "net zero" carbon dioxide emissions in
the middle of the second half of the century, approximately consistent
with the Paris Agreement's 2 degrees Celsius temperature target;4
Climate policy focused on deep decarbonization does not have to
characterize energy policy through the rest of the century --- it needs
instead to focus primarily on how to maintain, if not accelerate, rates
of decarbonization of recent decades for the next few decades; The U.S.
case shows that technological advancement is essential for maintaining
historical rates of decarbonization; So long as coal is on the grid,
natural gas is a bridge fuel --- Expanded nuclear electricity generation
would seem to be a no-brainer, for many reasons, and may in future
decades contribute to greater decarbonization; The U.S. natural gas boom
starting in the 2000s resulted from decisions made in the public and
private sectors (and in collaboration) since the 1970s. Energy policy
outcomes do not conform to electoral calendars; A question that should
be asked: Has U.S. climate policy since 1992 even mattered for
decarbonization? Or have steps that result in maintaining historical
rates of decarbonization been recharacterized as climate policy? A focus
on decarbonization has another benefit --- pretty much everyone values
economic growth, emissions reductions, or both. [Based on the historical
data, I'd hypothesize that economic growth and decarbonization are
actually **inter-related, and mutually reinforcing**]{.underline}.

#### Global decarbonization efforts are speeding up -- that will keep us under 2 degrees of warming

Ian **Stannard**, August **2024**, ICE, "Global decarbonization gaining
momentum?",
https://www.ice.com/insights/sustainable-finance/global-decarbonization-gaining-momentum,
mm

[Globally, the numbers of companies publishing]{.underline} temperature
targets and making emission reduction plans, such [as Net Zero 2050
commitments, has continued to increase]{.underline}. But is there any
evidence to suggest these commitments are having an impact on global
corporate emissions? [Our analysis]{.underline} using the ICE Global
Emissions and Targets Database to track global corporate emissions
[suggests there are positive signs.]{.underline} [We found improvement
in both the quality of data being reported and the trend in global
emission intensities, and absolute emissions globally]{.underline}.
Overall, [evidence points to the trend of global corporate
decarbonization **gaining momentum**]{.underline} in the last reporting
year. To carry out a detailed analysis and to examine progress on a
like-for-like basis we created a global diversified portfolio of
companies reporting complete Scope 1 and 2 emissions and at least one
category of Scope 3 emissions for at least the past two years (sample of
2,005 companies). The global average emission intensity (tCO2e/\$m
revenue) reduction for this sample portfolio is an impressive 30%
year-on-year for Scope 1 and 2 and 24% year-on-year for Scope 3 for the
last reporting year. Screening the portfolio for companies that also
publish a verified SBTi temperature target (sample 668 companies)
produces further interesting results. While the rate of emission
intensity reduction is not quite as steep for Scope 1 and 2 in the
latest reporting year at 26% year-on-year, the Scope 3 intensity decline
is 31% year-on-year. Overall average intensity levels for companies with
SBTi targets continue to be significantly below the average of the
broader sample portfolio, particularly for Scope 1 and 2 emissions.
These results are consistent with previous ICE research which found
companies with temperature targets tend to report lower intensities and
overall faster decarbonization rates than their peers without targets1.
In terms of absolute emissions (by EVIC), the declining trend is also
evident for Scope 1 and 2 emissions, for both the overall portfolio and
the SBTi screen portfolio. For Scope 3 absolute emissions the picture is
more complex. While there is no clear trend observed for overall Scope 3
emissions in recent years, we note divergent trends between the upstream
(declining) and downstream (rising) Scope 3 emissions for our sample
portfolio, a dynamic we will explore further in following articles.
Regional variations are also interesting. [European
companies]{.underline} on average [report the lowest emission
intensity]{.underline} for both Scope 1 and 2, and Scope 3 emissions,
[with continued strong decarbonization trends]{.underline} evident in
the latest data. At the other end of the scale, North American companies
have traditionally reported higher emission intensities, but here we are
finding encouraging trends emerging. In recent years, average Scope 1
and 2 emission intensities reported by North American companies have
fallen significantly. Average emission intensities reported by North
American companies are now aligned with the global average. However, it
is Scope 3 emission intensities where the most significant change
appears to be taking place. While average Scope 3 emission intensities
reported by North American companies remain the highest globally, these
have fallen dramatically and are now closing the gap with the rest of
the world. An assessment of the portfolio (before applying the SBTi
filter) using ICE's Net Zero Analysis tools, show 90% of companies in
the portfolio have published temperature targets (both verified and
unverified by SBTi). [Our analysis finds the portfolio is aligned to
the]{.underline} NGFS Phase IV [Net Zero 2050 scenario]{.underline}
until 2026, when some divergence starts to take place, giving an Implied
Temperature Rise (ITR) of 2.18 degrees C. [Applying the]{.underline}
SBTi \[[Science Based Targets initiative]{.underline}\] [filter improves
the performance further, taking the ITR down to 1.94 degrees
C]{.underline}. This is an improvement from 2.44 degrees C and 2.29
degrees C, respectively in the previous reporting year and is consistent
with the percentage reduction in the divergence of the portfolio
cumulative emissions from their Net Zero pathway.

#### The US has been actively [increasing]{.underline} its energy transition efforts.

Sami **Ulla and** Rundong **Luo,** 8/**23**, ScienceDirect, \[Sami Ulla
is a professor of biogeochemistry and geography at University of
Birmingham, and Rundong Luo is a professor of human resources and labor
economics at Shandong University\], "Advancing sustainable growth and
energy transition in the United States through the lens of green energy
innovations, natural resources and environmental policy,"
https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0301420723005597,
KR

The United States (US) is recognized as a major contributor to
greenhouse gas emissions globally, due to its extensive resource
utilization. However, sustainable development can be achieved with the
advent of technological advancements, resource management, and effective
environmental policy. This study evaluates the role of green energy
innovations (GENI), natural resources (NRSS), and environmental policy
(ENPY) in forecasting green growth and energy transition in the US. A
recently introduced Fourier quantile causality framework is used to
analyze the quarterly data ranges from 1990-Q1 to 2020-Q4. The results
indicate that [GENI, NRSS, and ENPY have **substantial** predictive
capabilities for green growth and **energy transition** in the
US]{.underline}, [particularly in higher quantiles, promoting
sustainable development.]{.underline} Additionally, [ENPY reinforces the
positive impacts of NRSS and GENI on energy transition and green
growth.]{.underline} The study highlights different policy points to
stimulate green growth and energy transition in the US to ensure
sustainable development.

Green growth and energy transition are critical components of achieving
sustainable development goals (SDGs) and ensuring a sustainable future
for our planet (Rahim et al., 2021). The world is facing significant
environmental challenges, such as climate change, resource depletion,
and pollution, which require urgent action (Wei et al., 2023). According
to the International Energy Agency (IEA), energy-associated carbon
emissions rose to a historic high of 33.2 gigatons in 2019, contributing
to the growing threat of climate change. However, there is a growing
recognition that transitioning to a low-carbon economy and promoting
green growth can provide significant economic, social, and environmental
benefits (Xie et al., 2022). A report by the IEA found that progressive
climate actions can create about \$26 trillion in economic benefits and
65 million new low-carbon jobs by 2030 (IEA, 2022). Most countries rely
on fossil fuels to meet their energy demands, which causes environmental
degradation and impeding the green growth (Afshan et al., 2022). The
current global economy necessitates a shift towards eco-friendly
practices and countries worldwide are striving to be carbon-free by
using advance technologies to promote green recovery. The green growth
is an essential indicator of a nation\'s ability to accomplish
sustainable development objectives in the long-term (Khan et al.,
2022a). Nonetheless, numerous factors such as technological innovations,
and renewable energy, have a significant impact on green growth (Khan et
al., 2021). It is therefore crucial for governments, businesses, and
individuals to prioritize green growth and energy transition to ensure a
sustainable future for generations to come.

[The US]{.underline} has long been one of the world\'s significant
consumers of energy and natural resources, with soaring economic growth
and industrialization driving significant demand. However, this
consumption has come at a cost, with significant environmental impacts,
including greenhouse gas emissions, pollution, and resource depletion
(Zafar et al., 2019). As such, promoting sustainable development and
transitioning to a sustainable/green energy system is crucial for the
US\'s long-term economic and environmental health. However, in recent
years, the country [has made significant strides in **reducing** its
energy and resource consumption while maintaining economic growth.
Between 2005 and 2019, the US **declined** its energy intensity by
**19.1%**]{.underline}, while the economy grew by 25% (Yang et al.,
2021). Additionally, renewable energy has become increasingly
competitive, with the cost of wind and solar power falling rapidly in
recent years. According to a report by the IEA, costs of wind and solar
energy have dropped by 70% and 89% by 2010 in the US, respectively.
These trends demonstrate that economic growth and energy consumption are
not mutually exclusive until the percentage of renewable energy
resources are not integrated in the energy mix. By transitioning to a
more sustainable energy system, the US can mitigate its environmental
effect alongside preserving economic growth. Furthermore, promoting
sustainable development can create new economic opportunities,
particularly in the rapidly growing clean energy sector. As such, it is
crucial that the country continues to prioritize sustainable growth and
energy transition as part of its long-term economic strategy to ensure a
sustainable future.

Among the many other institutional, economic, and financial factors,
green energy innovations are becoming increasingly vital for sustainable
growth and the energy transition (Chen and Lei, 2018). As the world is
facing the severe challenges of climate change, rising energy demands,
and the exhaustion of natural resources, it\'s crucial to find
alternative sources of energy that are renewable and environmentally
friendly (Wei et al., 2023). Green energy innovations, such as wind
turbines, solar panels, and hydroelectric power, provide sustainable
solutions to the world\'s energy needs (Afshan et al., 2022). Innovative
technologies and business models can help to drive down the cost of
renewable energy, increase energy efficiency, and improve energy storage
and distribution systems, thus promoting sustainable development
(Petrariu et al., 2013; Wen et al., 2022). Not only do they reduce
carbon emissions, but they also promote energy independence and create
new economic opportunities including the creation of new industries and
jobs in the rapidly growing clean energy sector (Saleem et al., 2020).
The development and application of green energy innovations have the
ability to transform the process we produce and consume energy, inducing
a sustainable future (Xie et al., 2022). Governments, businesses, and
individuals alike must invest in and promote green energy innovations to
drive the energy transition forward and diminish the influences of
climate change because it is crucial to continue prioritizing their
development and implementation to promote sustainable development
(Ramzan et al., 2022).

Likewise, natural resources are at the core of sustainable growth and
energy transition. The abundance and efficient utilization of natural
resources like wind, hydro, solar, and biomass are crucial in ensuring a
transition to clean and sustainable energy (Jhariya et al., 2022). On
the positive side, natural resources offer a clean and renewable
resource of energy, declining our reliance on non-renewable energy
resources and helping to mitigate climate change (Abbasi et al., 2021;
Lee and He, 2022), which in turn improves public health and helps to
secure a stable environment. Furthermore, natural resources are the key
source of raw material used in producing renewable energy technologies
like wind turbines, batteries, and solar panels (Wang et al., 2021). For
instance, the production of solar panels needs materials like silicon,
glass, and aluminum, while wind turbines require steel and rare earth
metals. However, extracting and using natural resources can have
significant environmental and social impacts. For instance, mining
operations can result in deforestation, soil erosion, water pollution,
and habitat destruction (Mela et al., 2021). Furthermore, producing some
renewable technologies like batteries generate significant amounts of
waste and pollution (Huang, 2022). It is crucial to manage natural
resources\' sustainability and to minimize the climate and social
impacts associated with their extraction, production, and use for a
sustainable growth. In addition, natural resources are finite, and their
availability is not evenly distributed worldwide. This means that there
are geopolitical implications associated with their use, including
issues related to access, control, and distribution (Gyamfi et al.,
2022). To address these challenges, it is important to prioritize
sustainable resource management, promote resource efficiency, and invest
in the development of alternative and more sustainable production
methods.

Environmental policies are also essential for promoting green growth and
facilitating the transition to a sustainable energy structure(Shao et
al., 2021). These policies can help to address some of the most
significant environmental and social challenges facing the world today,
such as climate change, air and water pollution, and biodiversity loss
(Dechezleprêtre and Sato, 2017; Ibrahim and Ajide, 2021). At the same
time, the implementation of these policies can also have both favorable
and adverse effects on economic growth, social equity, and individual
behavior. On the positive side, effective environmental policies can
create new economic opportunities in the clean energy sector, generating
employment and supporting local businesses. For instance, policies such
as feed-in tariffs and tax incentives for renewable energy installations
can encourage investment in sustainable technologies and promote
innovation in the clean energy sector (Razzaq et al., 2021).
Furthermore, environmental policies can lead to improved public health
outcomes by reducing air and water pollution and mitigating the impacts
of climate change (Dechezleprêtre and Sato, 2017). However,
environmental policies can also have negative consequences, particularly
for industries that are heavily reliant on fossil fuels or other
non-renewable resources (Borozan, 2018). Such policies can lead to job
losses, particularly in regions where these industries are a significant
source of employment (Dogan et al., 2023). Also, environmental policies
may not always be socially equitable, as the costs of transition may be
disproportionately borne by low-income or marginalized communities
(Bashir et al., 2021). Therefore, it is vital to formulate and implement
environmental policies in a way that balances economic growth, social
equity, and environmental protection.

The US faces significant challenges in achieving sustainable growth and
energy transition while maintaining its position as a global economic
leader. To address these challenges, it is essential to prioritize
innovation in green energy technologies, effective usage of natural
resources, and efficient environmental policies to realize the
sustainable development. Innovation in renewable energy technologies can
provide a foundation for sustainable energy systems while promoting
economic growth. Recent data shows that [in 2022, renewable energy
sources, including wind and solar, provided **24%** of US electricity
generation, up from **10%** in 2010 (EIA, 2023).]{.underline}
Encouragingly, [the US government has announced plans **to invest \$35
billion in clean energy research and development** over the next decade
(IEA, 2022).]{.underline} In addition, [the US]{.underline} has vast
natural resources that can support the transition to a sustainable
energy system, such as solar, wind, biomass, and geothermal. However,
the country also faces significant challenges related to natural
resource management, including the potential environmental impacts of
resource extraction, land use conflicts, and access issues (Zafar et
al., 2019). To overcome these challenges and achieve a sustainable
energy transition, the US needs to execute some sound environmental
policies. For instance, [the country has implemented **a national
renewable energy standard** and **carbon pricing mechanisms**, such as
the **Regional Greenhouse Gas Initiative**, which have helped to
**incentivize** the implementation of **renewable technologies and
reduce emissions.**]{.underline} Fig. 1 uncovers the trends of
sustainable growth (SGRW), energy transition (ENGT), green energy
innovations (GENI), natural resources (NRSS), and environmental policy
(ENPY) in the US for the period 1990 to 2020. The figure exposes huge
variations in the sustainable growth of the US across the years;
however, all the factors are seen to have an increasing trend. This
gives us a hint that [the US is significantly **promoting innovations in
green energy, natural resources, and environmental policies** over time,
**progressing towards sustainable growth** and **energy
transition**.]{.underline} Therefore, it is essential to prioritize
green energy innovations, natural resources, and environmental policies,
and this study aims to empirically examine the significance of these
factors for sustainable growth and energy transition in the US while
promoting economic growth, social equity, and environmental protection
to realize sustainable development.

This study offers a significant contribution to the prevailing
literature by examining the dynamics of the relationship between green
energy innovations, natural resources, environmental policy, sustainable
growth, and energy transition in the US. Using a recently developed
Fourier quantile causality test, the study offers unique insights into
the association among these variables at different quantiles, providing
a more detailed and robust understanding of their interactions. This
study is novel in several ways. Firstly, it is the first to examine the
relationship between green energy innovations, natural resources, and
environmental policy with sustainable growth and energy transition in
the US, using a robust econometric technique and the latest data.
Secondly, the study also investigates the moderating influence of
environmental policy on the relationship of green energy innovations and
natural resources with sustainable growth and energy transition, which
have not been taken into consideration previously. Finally, the study
has used a Fourier quantile causality test in conjunction with spectrum
analysis, allowing for the investigation of causal links among the
variables in short-, medium-, and long-term based on different
quantiles. This test has yielded reliable outcomes since it considers
structural breakdowns, quantity, position, and shape that are not needed
to predetermine. The technique employed in this study is innovative, and
it provides policymakers with valuable information on the actual
magnitude of each covariate, helping to develop suitable policies for
sustainable growth and energy transition in the US economy. The outcomes
from the [**empirical analysis** revealed **a positive role** of natural
resources, green energy innovations, and environmental policy in
promoting green growth and energy transition in the US.]{.underline}
More importantly, [an efficient environmental policy strengthens the
influence of green energy innovations and natural resources on green
growth and energy transition, ensuring the significance of these factors
for a sustainable future in the US.]{.underline} These results provide
policymakers with valuable insights into the most effective policies and
strategies to promote sustainable development.

#### The global energy transition is succeeding now

Will **Atkinson** **and** Chiara **Gulli**, '**25** Bachelor of Arts
Major in Geosciences; Certificate in Environmental Studies, and key
strategist on the energy transition,  [rmi.org](http://rmi.org), 1/8/25,
'The Energy Transition in 2025: What To Watch For',
https://rmi.org/the-energy-transition-in-2025-what-to-watch-for///LPiatek

[Despite warnings of a slowdown, solutions continue to race
forward]{.underline}. As cleantech becomes cheaper than ever, [2024 saw
record uptake in renewable energy]{.underline}, electric vehicles (EVs),
and more. [These positive tipping points are happening
worldwide]{.underline} --- with major progress in China and the Global
South. Let's review what that means for the year ahead. Costs falling
fast. Solar module prices fell 35 percent to 9 cents per watt; EV
batteries are now below \$100/kWh and often at cost parity with their
fossil-fueled competition. Cleantech growing globally. Solar additions
grew to 600 GW, EV sales climbed 25 percent, and battery storage
additions nearly doubled. Changing perception. [IEA forecasts have
improved for cleantech and fallen for fossil fuels]{.underline} several
years in a row. New year, new progress? Energy efficiency and methane
are the two fastest ways to cut warming, but also the two furthest off
track.From ambition to action. With national climate plans due in
February, it is time to include all sectors, pollutants, and solutions
--- and then hit the ground running.After 2024, clean energy is cheaper
than ever. Global solar module prices fell 35 percent to less than 9
cents/kWh. EV batteries saw their best price decline in seven years,
dropping \~30--50 percent for cathodes and 20 percent for the full
battery to below \$100/kWh. EVs are now at cost-of-ownership parity in
the United States and purchase price parity in China --- with that
milestone expected around now for Europe, in 2026 for the United States,
and in 2027 for India's two- and four-wheelers. Thankfully, lower cost
does not mean lower quality. Take batteries: the average cell in 2024
used less than half as much nickel and cobalt as a decade ago, and new
technologies could double energy densities in the next five years. As we
see improving safety, charge time, and longevity, uptake will follow and
drive down cost in a virtuous cycle. [Cleantech uptake is more
widespread than ever.]{.underline} Renewable energy additions grew 17
percent with a record \~600 GW of solar, \~125 GW of wind, and
near-doubling of grid storage installations to \~170 GWh in 2024.
Renewables now outpace fossil electricity investment by 10 to 1, with
more investment in solar than all other power sources combined. As a
result, [renewables are poised to overtake coal as the leading power
source in 2025.]{.underline} [This progress is truly
global]{.underline}. As a share of electricity, solar and wind is
scaling twice as fast in the Global South as in the Global North.
Countries like Pakistan and Namibia have used Chinese solar exports to
nearly double their total electricity capacity in just two years.

### UQ -- AT Trump Climate Policy

#### Macro forces outweigh -- Trump's impact will be limited

David **Keith**, 1/22/**25**, SRM360, \[Professor of Geophysical Science
at the Univ. of Chicago and founding Faculty Director at the Climate
Systems Engineering Initiative\], "What Do the Announcements from the
New U.S. Administration Mean for SRM?"
https://srm360.org/news-reaction/new-us-administration-srm/, mm

Trump brings worries right up to the chance that an impulsive president
blunders into nuclear war. So, I am not panicked about Trump's climate
impact. It's true that [Trump's]{.underline} [energy]{.underline} and
climate [announcements are foolish]{.underline} and deliberately
provocative[, but it's unlikely they will have a big
impact]{.underline}. [Global CO2 emissions are set to peak within the
next few years]{.underline}. [The US is]{.underline} important, but it's
[only about 14% of global CO2 emissions]{.underline}. [A presidential
term is just four years, and climate is driven by cumulative emissions
over decades.]{.underline} And finally, [Trump's executive orders can't
do much to change the trajectory of US energy investments]{.underline}.

#### Trump's policies won't affect the energy transition.

Conor **Harrison,** 4/29/**25**, Royal Geographical Society, \[Conor
Harrison is the head of the Department of Geography and School of Earth,
Ocean and Environment in University of South Carolina\], "Trump and the
US energy transition,"
https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009, KR

While the electricity industry is responsible for 25% of US greenhouse
gas emissions, it is also regarded as the easiest sector to decarbonise
and foundational to subsequent decarbonisation via vehicle, home and
industrial electrification (Lawson,
[2018](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0010)).
[The growth of renewable energy in the **U**nited **S**tates during the
last two decades has been **remarkable**. Renewables are no longer a
fringe part of the energy system. Renewable energy firms **employ
millions of workers** in both installation and manufacturing, and the
industry **generates billions of dollars** in fees and tax credits for
some of the world\'s largest financial institutions]{.underline} (Job
Creation & Economic Growth,
[n.d.](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0007);
Knuth,
[2021](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0009)).
On some particularly windy or sunny days, [**renewables provide the
majority of electricity** that courses through the electricity grid
in]{.underline} places like [Texas, Iowa and California.]{.underline} It
is important to note that energy transitions are not just technical
processes. They are also social and economic processes embedded within
our highly unequal society. Energy systems are highly uneven, featuring
multiple territorialisations of energy politics and governance. They are
systems in which remnants of the old come into conflict with the new,
meaning that transitions are less about the replacement of one energy
source with another, than the lumpy and contentious recombination and
reconfiguration of energy sources, forms of governance, and corporate
accumulation strategies (Bridge & Gailing,
[2020](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0003);
Harrison,
[2022](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0006);
Welton,
[2021](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0024)).
In the United States, huge sunk costs in various forms of energy
extraction and electricity generation have made the electricity industry
very slow to change. Moreover, the federal government has traditionally
taken the backseat in energy governance, with the lion\'s share of
electricity policy made at the state level (Boyd & Carlson,
[2016](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0002)).
This means electricity governance varies substantially from state to
state, with some states leaving electricity operations to vertically
integrated monopolies while others are part of 'competitive' regional
wholesale markets that procure power from independent generators. The
last two decades have seen three trends shape the US electricity
industry. First, the use of cheap fracked gas for electricity generation
has increased dramatically to the point that it accounts for
approximately 40% of electricity generation. Second, [natural gas and
renewables have gradually pushed coal out of the generation mix, down to
around 16% in 2023.]{.underline} Third, and finally, electricity demand
has remained largely stagnant, meaning that the size of the electricity
generation pie has largely remained the same even as the ingredients
have been reconfigured (US Energy Information Administration,
[2023](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0022);
Seiple,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0020)).
The rise of renewable energy has been shaped less by the quality of the
resource and more by federal and especially state-level policy, namely
renewable portfolio standards and/or tax incentives (e.g., for many
years cloudy Massachusetts had more installed solar capacity than sunny
Florida or Arizona). But this has begun to change in recent years as
[the falling cost of installing wind and solar has made renewables
attractive to large power buyers, especially when buyers are further
incentivised by generous tax credits and corporate pledges to move to
100% clean and/or renewable energy]{.underline} (Christophers,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0004);
Knuth,
[2021](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0009)).
[This configuration of renewable energy developers, state-level
renewable requirements, and corporate renewable energy
buyers]{.underline} (i.e., Meta, Google, Amazon) [began to coalesce
during Trump\'s first term but was enhanced by the passage of the
IRA]{.underline} by the US Congress in 2022. To attract support from the
politically powerful electric utility industry, the Act is packed with
incentives but very few penalties on carbon emitters---all carrots and
no sticks. [The prime incentives in the IRA are tax
credits]{.underline}. [The Act increased the magnitude of already
available federal tax credits and extended the length of the programme
**through 2032, providing certainty** for renewable
developers]{.underline} (Martin,
[2022](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0012)).
Recent analysis indicates that [**IRA has ramped up renewable energy
generation**, with corresponding greenhouse gas reductions,]{.underline}
though not to levels required to meet US obligations in the Paris
Agreement (Rhodium Group & MIT CEEPR,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0019)).
Beyond tax credits for renewable energy generation, [the IRA also
includes tax incentives for moving 'clean' manufacturing (i.e., solar
panels, batteries, and electric vehicles) to the United States. Since
the passage of the IRA, **hundreds of new manufacturing facilities**
have been announced in the United States., a **majority in** reliably
**Republican states**]{.underline} (Bermel et al.,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0001)).
Indeed, so much manufacturing has moved, or is planning to move, to the
United States that---in combination with the rapid growth of data
centres supporting energy-intensive artificial intelligence -- [electric
utilities are now facing the prospect of growing electricity demand for
the first time in decades]{.underline} (Seiple,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0020)).
Given this scenario, many utilities are eyeing major new investments in
generation, and for some, new natural gas generators, a controversial
but long-lasting solution to their pending generation crunch. This
dynamic makes the coming years even more important to the
decarbonisation of the sector. To begin, there are some Biden-era
policies that Trump can undo by executive order, but [most changes will
rely on Congress]{.underline}, where Republicans hold a slim majority in
both houses. [This]{.underline} likely [makes **broad sweeping change
difficult.**]{.underline} Second, cabinet appointments in the
Departments of Energy and Interior are important for the trajectory of
the energy industry, but because of the heavy reliance of the IRA on tax
credits, the Department of the Treasury---which has issued nearly 100
pieces of guidance on energy tax credit qualifications---may be of
greater importance (Plautz,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0018)).
Third, and relatedly, [Trump treats the stock markets as an important
barometer of a proposed policy\'s popularity]{.underline} (Swan &
Haberman,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0021)),
[meaning that **the financial industry may provide additional guardrails
that will restrain the new administration\'s energy
policy.**]{.underline} With these elements in mind, early reporting from
and conversations with the electricity *and* financial industry press,
consultants and advisory firms point to some likely outcomes. In terms
of fossil fuels, there is no reason to believe that the production of
natural gas in the United States will slow. If anything, there might be
a coming glut over-production. The outlook for coal is less rosy.
Trump\'s first presidential campaign in 2016 featured him wearing a hard
hat and promising to resuscitate the coal industry. Trump did not save
coal the first time because there is not much that can be done to save
the coal industry. The only hope for the coal industry is that
electricity demand growth and regulatory roll-back might delay the
retirement dates scheduled for many of the coal plants across the United
States (Frazin,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0005)).
The question, then, is whether electric utilities will meet new
electricity demand by building new natural gas generation or whether
they will turn towards renewables, storage and/or nuclear. And much of
that decision-making rests on the fate of the IRA. During a
post-election roundtable discussion featuring a group of Republican
Party energy insiders, one commentator framed the IRA issue as having
two sides. On one side are conservative and climate-change denying
Republican lawmakers that staunchly oppose it, viewing it as part of
Biden\'s 'woke' green agenda. More than this, they see a full repeal of
the IRA as an important revenue source to fund their ultimate objective
of extending Trump\'s 2017 tax cuts, which are set to expire at the end
of 2025 (Martin,
[2024a](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0013)).[1](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-note-0002_24)
Lawmakers on the other side of the issue are Democrats and a small but
influential group of Republicans who---like the signees of the NAM
letter---point to the renewable energy and clean manufacturing projects
spurred by the IRA, many of which are located in Republican states.
Given the slim Republican control over Congress, [the most likely
outcome is that the IRA will be picked apart by a scalpel, not beaten
with a sledgehammer]{.underline} (Martin,
[2024a](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0013)).
Most commentators agree that the most politically vulnerable parts are
subsidies for electric vehicles, followed by programmes that provide tax
credits for residential energy efficiency and electrification upgrades
like heat pumps (Martucci,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0015);
Pearl et al.,
[n.d.](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0017)).
Also highly vulnerable are offshore wind projects---the subject of many
Trump polemics on the campaign trail---although [the administration has
little ability to stop projects that are farther along the development
path.]{.underline} However, many of these projects are already in
jeopardy due to rising costs (Kinniburgh,
[2024](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0008)).
The situation for onshore [wind and solar]{.underline} is better. Recall
that this industry is supported by a variety of policy and financial
forces---state-level renewable portfolio standards, demand from
corporate buyers, and financial institutions eager to acquire tax
credits. It also [benefits from political and economic momentum and
support in both Democratic]{.underline} (e.g., California and New York)
[and Republican]{.underline} (e.g., Texas and Florida)
[strongholds]{.underline}. Importantly, [utilities in the United States
largely support the IRA and are **loath to pivot their strategies**
every four years based on political outcomes]{.underline} (Lipton,
[2022](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0011);
Martin,
[2024b](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0014)).
[Early indications are that **utilities will stand by their development
pipelines**, most of which are packed full of solar, wind and storage.
Even if Republicans **claw back some of the money** from the IRA by
shortening the time frame for tax credits, **this would have little
impact on projects already under development, sustaining the industry
through at least the next four to five years.** Indeed, many developers
are currently ensuring their projects are far enough along to be
grandfathered in should any changes to the IRA occur]{.underline}
(Martin,
[2024b](https://rgs-ibg.onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/full/10.1111/geoj.70009#geoj70009-bib-0014)).

### Link -- Delays the Transition

Uta **Klönne**, Carl-Friedrich **Schleussner**, **and** Bill **Hare**,

#### Geoengineering creates a moral hazard -- it will gut any public or political pressure to pursue decarbonization

Albert **Lin**, **2013**, Ecology Law Quarterly, 40(673), "Does
geoengineering present a moral hazard?",
https://law.ucdavis.edu/sites/g/files/dgvnsk10866/files/media/documents/ELQ.MoralHazard.pdf,
mm

How is geoengineering likely to be framed amidst substantial climate
skepticism? Relatedly, and more pertinent to assessing the moral hazard
posed by geoengineering, how is geoengineering likely to be perceived?
Like mitigation efforts, geoengineering operations cannot be justified
without an acknowledgment that climate change is occurring. Accordingly,
one might initially expect opponents of mitigation to be hostile to
geoengineering as well. [[Geoengineering offers a]{.mark} very
[different]{.mark} economic [calculation]{.mark}]{.underline}, however,
for the companies that would bear much of the cost of climate
mitigation. Utilities, oil companies, and other [[carbon-intensive
industries would benefit]{.mark} from persuading the public that
geoengineering offers a relatively inexpensive and painless option for
addressing climate change]{.underline}. A climate policy dominated by
geoengineering would facilitate continuation of business as usual,
enabling the avoidance---or at least deferral---of fundamental and
potentially costly changes to current industry practices. Some companies
might even develop a direct financial stake in designing and
implementing geoengineering schemes For purposes of analyzing public
perceptions of geoengineering, the public may fall into two camps
according to their underlying views on climate change: believers and
skeptics. Persons in both groups are subject to heuristics and biases
that foster overconfidence in geoengineering's efficacy and
underweighting of geoengineering's risks.208 [[Cultural cognition theory
suggests]{.underline},]{.mark} moreover, [that climate change
[skeptics]{.mark} [are]{.mark} especially [likely to experience moral
hazard]{.mark} with respect to geoengineering]{.underline}. This
prediction may appear counterintuitive at first, as there is presumably
no need for climate change policy measures---geoengineering or
otherwise---if climate change is not a problem to begin with. But as
cultural cognition theory predicts, the policy options offered in
response to a risk can substantially alter public perceptions of that
risk.209 Specifically, [for climate]{.underline} change
[skeptics]{.underline} who have resisted climate mitigation,
[geoengineering offers a policy option more consonant with culturally
conservative values]{.underline}. Supporting evidence exists in studies
examining the influence of cultural values on perceptions of another
controversial technology, nuclear power. Persons with individualistic
and hierarchical orientations ("hierarchical individualists") tend to be
the strongest supporters of nuclear power, whereas persons with more
communitarian and egalitarian orientations tend to be its strongest
opponents.210 Not surprisingly, the former group also tends to be
relatively skeptical of global warming. When nuclear power is framed as
a possible solution to global warming, however, hierarchical
individualists have been found to be more open to evidence of global
warming.211 For these persons, geoengineering could have an appeal
similar to that of nuclear energy.212 By illustrating how technology
might solve problems faced by humanity, geoengineering could serve as an
affirmation of human initiative, capitalism, and scientific progress.213
[The possibility that one might simultaneously deny climate change and
advocate geoengineering is not merely theoretical. The Cato Institute
has derided]{.underline} [concern]{.underline} [over]{.underline} global
[warming]{.underline} as a "scare" [while framing opposition to
geoengineering as opposition to]{.underline} economic
[growth]{.underline}.214 [Views expressed by]{.underline} scholars
associated with [the American Enterprise Institute]{.underline} (AEI), a
conservative think-tank, [provide a further illustration]{.underline}.
The AEI has financed attacks on climate change science and sponsored
programs critical of international efforts to combat climate change and
other environmental problems.215 At the same time, AEI scholars also
have expressed strong support for geoengineering research and
deployment. Testifying before the House Committee on Science and
Technology, Lee Lane, co-director of AEI's geoengineering project,
characterized stratospheric aerosols and other similarly speculative SRM
techniques as "very likely to be a feasible and effective means of
cooling the planet."216 Advocating that SRM be viewed no differently
than any other policy tool for responding to climate change, Lane
blithely suggested that SRM "may have more upside potential than does
any other climate policy option."217 Samuel Thernstrom, the other
co-director of AEI's geoengineering project, more recently cautioned
that "\[g\]eoengineering should be seen as a complement to mitigation
and adaptation, not an alternative," and deemed it "implausible that any
national leader would argue that geoengineering offers a safe
alternative to emissions reductions---or that the American people would
go along with the idea."218 Nonetheless, Thernstrom touted
geoengineering for its "unique ability to overcome the inertia in the
climate system and provide a degree of rapid cooling, if necessary," and
advocated immediate research on geoengineering.219 The knowledge thereby
gained, he contended, "would be relatively cheap and potentially
priceless, while continued ignorance of this field would be reckless . .
. ."220 Perhaps unsurprisingly, the proponents of these views make
little mention of geoengineering's drawbacks. They also gloss over the
tremendous difficulties of developing effective geoengineering
techniques and determining whether they would actually work.
[[Deployment of any serious geoengineering project is]{.mark} estimated
to be [decades away]{.mark}, even if research efforts were accelerated
immediately and even if such efforts ultimately proved
successful.]{.underline}221 Simply put, [[geoengineering offers no magic
bullet]{.underline}]{.mark}. [Efforts to frame [geoengineering]{.mark}
as a necessary]{.underline} choice under desperate circumstances
nevertheless [[may exacerbate the moral hazard
effect]{.mark}.]{.underline} [Proponents]{.underline} [of
geoengineering]{.underline} research have [described
[geoengineering]{.mark} as "the]{.underline} [only]{.underline} human
[response that can fend off rapid]{.underline} and high-consequence
[climate impacts]{.underline}."222 [Such [statements]{.mark} [shift
attention away from mitigation]{.mark} by "play\[ing\] on the fears of
the public and advocat\[ing\] technological quick-fixes rather than
reasonable debate about instituting difficult changes]{.underline} to
our resource-based and extractive mode of existence."223 The ease with
which geoengineering supporters sometimes make their arguments
underscores the potentially widespread psychological and political
appeal of geoengineering. [Because it is susceptible to framing as a
magic bullet against climate change, geoengineering may prove
attractive]{.underline} not only to persons whose cultural values align
with geoengineering, but also [to the broader American
public.]{.underline} [[Studies find that Americans strongly support GHG
emission reductions, yet tend to oppose]{.underline}]{.mark} specific
[[policies that would discourage fossil fuel
consumption]{.underline}.]{.mark}224 Interpreting this apparent
contradiction, the author of one such study suggests that American
public opinion on climate change is "in a 'wishful thinking' stage of
opinion formation, in which they hope the problem can be solved by
someone else (government, industry, etc.), without changes in their own
priorities, decision making or behavior."225
[[Geoengineering]{.underline}]{.mark}, in hinting at a cheap and easy
resolution---or at least postponement---of our climate reckoning,
[[plays directly into this wishful thinking]{.underline}]{.mark}. Even
if [[politicians]{.underline}]{.mark} recognize the problems associated
with geoengineering, they [[face little incentive to dispel]{.mark} any
[illusions]{.mark} the public may hold]{.underline}. Rather than make
difficult choices that impose costs on the electorate, [it will be
easier for elected officials to point to a technological fix that may
one day arrive, obviating the need for sacrifice or a departure from
business as usual]{.underline}.226 By suggesting additional potential
options for responding to climate change, [[geoengineering reduces the
political pressure for near-term mitigation and provides opponents of
mitigation with a new rationale for further
delay]{.underline}]{.mark}.227 In the end, people might want so much to
believe that geoengineering will work that they may allow politicians
and interested parties to convince them that it will work, regardless of
evidence to the contrary.
