### Impact - Decarbonization Solves Climate

#### Energy transition key to stopping climate change

**NDEP , 25** (United Nations Development Program, world\'s largest
initiative to support countries in enhancing their national climate
pledges, known as Nationally Determined Contributions (NDCs), under the
Paris Agreement, UNDP Climate Promise,
2/3/25,<https://climatepromise.undp.org/news-and-stories/what-sustainable-energy-transition-and-why-it-key-tackling-climate-change>//LPiatek

[**The sustainable energy transition** **is a transformative shift in
how energy is produced, distributed and consumed, aiming to move away
from fossil fuels towards a system centred on renewable energy
sources**]{.underline} such as solar, wind, hydropower and geothermal.
[This **shift is**]{.underline} **[critical to tackling the climate
crisis](https://www.ipcc.ch/report/ar6/wg3/chapter/chapter-6/)[,]{.underline}**
[as fossil fuels still provide]{.underline} [80 percent of global energy
supply](https://www.iea.org/data-and-statistics/data-tools/greenhouse-gas-emissions-from-energy-data-explorer)[,
releasing immense amounts of planet-warming gases, such as]{.underline}
[carbon
dioxide](https://climate.nasa.gov/vital-signs/carbon-dioxide/?intent=121)
[and]{.underline}
[methane](https://www.iea.org/reports/global-methane-tracker-2024)[, in
the process.At **it**s core, the sustainable energy transition **seeks
to** transform the global energy system to **combat climate change while
fostering sustainable development.**]{.underline} **It involves** not
only **adopting cleaner energy sources** but also **enhancing energy
efficiency,** deploying advanced technologies like energy storage, and
**decarbonizing key sectors** such as electricity, transportation and
industry[.However, **beyond being a tech**nological **shift**, **this
transition** is a transformative process that **prioritizes equity,
inclusion and human development. Central to the sustainable energy
transition is the concept of a**]{.underline} **[just
transition](https://climatepromise.undp.org/news-and-stories/what-just-transition-and-why-it-important)[,
ensuring fairness and inclusion for all, particularly for those in
fossil-fuel intensive industries who stand to lose jobs and income and
communities who are more vulnerable to rising costs of living and energy
insecurity.Therefore, the sustainable energy transition not only
delivers environmental benefits but also drives innovation, creates jobs
and reduces energy poverty]{.underline}**[.]{.underline} It represents a
unique opportunity to address common global challenges like [energy
access
disparities](https://www.worldbank.org/en/news/press-release/2024/06/11/progress-on-basic-energy-access-reverses-for-first-time-in-a-decade),
[energy
security](https://www.iea.org/reports/world-energy-outlook-2022/energy-security-in-energy-transitions),
[inequality](https://data.undp.org/blog/1-18-billion-around-the-world-in-energy-poverty)
and [health
impacts](https://climatepromise.undp.org/news-and-stories/climate-crisis-health-crisis-heres-why)
through integrated and transformative solutions, **laying the foundation
for a sustainable, inclusive and resilient future**.Why does the
sustainable energy transition matter for climate action[?The current
energy system is a major driver of global climate change, accounting for
around]{.underline} [75 percent of total greenhouse gas
emissions](https://www.wri.org/insights/4-charts-explain-greenhouse-gas-emissions-countries-and-sectors)[.]{.underline}
As temperatures break records [year after
year](https://climate.copernicus.eu/global-climate-highlights-2024),
**transforming how we produce and consume energy is essential**.Under
the [Paris
Agreement](https://unfccc.int/process-and-meetings/the-paris-agreement),
a legally binding international treaty on climate change, countries have
committed to deep, rapid and sustained reductions in greenhouse gas
emissions to limit global warming to well below 2° C above
pre-industrial levels, preferably to 1.5° C. The world's ability to meet
this goal hinges on the energy sector's ability to reach [net-zero
emissions by 2050](https://www.ipcc.ch/sr15/chapter/spm/).The case for
action has never been stronger. The landmark [decision from the first
Global Stocktake](https://unfccc.int/documents/636608), adopted at
[COP28 in Dubai](https://unfccc.int/cop28) in 2023, outlines
transformative energy-related pathways and approaches that countries
must pursue, including: Tripling global renewable energy capacity and
doubling the global annual average rate of energy efficiency
improvements by 2030; Transitioning away from fossil fuels in energy
systems, in a just, orderly and equitable manner; **Phasing out
inefficient fossil fuel subsidies that do not address energy poverty or
just transitions.T[he decision highlights the urgency to accelerate
global efforts towards a net-zero emission energy system, one that
produces little or no carbon emissions from fossil fuels and industrial
processes, before 2050.]{.underline}**

### Petrostates ! - AT Petrostates Aren't Aggressive

#### Robust empirical research supports the connection between petrostates and conflict

Jeff **Colgan**, **2021**, (Colgan is Richard Holbrooke Associate
Professor of Political Science in the Department of Political Science
and the Watson Institute of Public and International Affairs at Brown
University), Security Studies, 30(4), "Oil and War: An Exchange,"
https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/09636412.2021.1984671, mm

Hye Ryeon [Jang]{.underline} [and]{.underline} Benjamin
[Smith]{.underline} [argue]{.underline} in their article "Pax Petrolica?
Rethinking the Oil--Interstate War Linkage" [that]{.underline}, when
reexamined, [the historical data are "more supportive of
petro-peace]{.underline} than of petro-aggression." Their argument is a
direct critique of my research on that topic, which includes my book
Petro-Aggression: When Oil Causes War. They have a number of concerns,
but their central claim is that "the Iran--Iraq War is singularly
responsible for what was believed to have been a radical
petro-aggression effect globally." In various statistical models, they
add a dummy variable for the eighteen country-year observations
associated with the Iran--Iraq War (1980--88) and argue that this
reveals a key variable from my research to be statistically
insignificant. The key variable is petro-revolutionary, a dichotomous
indicator for a state that is both a "petrostate" (defined as a state
with net oil exports of at least 10% of gross domestic product (GDP))
and has a revolutionary government. The tendency of petro-revolutionary
states to instigate interstate conflicts at a high rate is the
phenomenon I label "petro-aggression." Jang and Smith engage with my
research on the topic in a serious way. [I do not find their critique
compelling]{.underline}, but I am grateful for the chance to reevaluate
the evidence from new perspectives. [Their critique might actually add
to the confidenc]{.underline}e we can have in the idea of
[petro-aggression]{.underline} rather than reduce it. There are six
reasons why: first, we probably should not see the Iran--Iraq War as an
outlier; second, even if we do, there are different ways of dealing with
outliers that statisticians typically prefer to the method Jang and
Smith use, and those methods generate different results; third, even if
we accept their method, their results do not justify affirming the null
hypothesis; fourth, their other concerns about measures, when tested,
actually provide empirical support for petro-aggression; fifth, the
qualitative historical evidence adds further support; and sixth, when
judging robustness, we should consider the full range of empirical tests
rather than a highly selective focus. [First, it is not at all clear
that the Iran--Iraq War is an outlier]{.underline}. Standard econometric
texts repeatedly warn analysts away from treating observations too
swiftly as outliers. It is safe to do so only rarely: for example, if
survey data indicated the age of a respondent as 150 years, we could
view that as an outlier, probably an error, because there are zero known
cases of humans living that long. The Iran--Iraq War was not that kind
of event at all. We know it existed; it is not an error. It was a major
war with enormous casualties on both sides, precisely the kind of event
that people studying the causes of war hope to understand and prevent.
Jang and Smith provide relatively little justification for treating it
as an outlier. [They are correct that the war produced a lot of
militarized interstate disputes]{.underline} (MIDs), [but big wars do
that]{.underline}. After all, the Iran--Iraq War was the longest
conventional interstate war of the twentieth century, and it took place
at the heart of the global oil system, so it involved many players. Iran
instigated 62 MIDs in the 1980--88 period and Iraq instigated 35,
meaning a rate of 6.9 and 3.9 MIDs per year, respectively. That is a
lot, but still a lower rate than Germany's 53 MIDs between 1939 and
1944, a rate of 8.2 per year. The Iran--Iraq War stands out because few
big wars occurred in the period the dataset covers (1945--2001), but
**[historically such wars do happen]{.underline}**. We should not treat
big wars as outliers simply because they produce a lot of MIDs. The term
"outlier" might actually be confusing. Although the Iran--Iraq War might
be an outlier in the quantitative sense that it generated a lot of MIDs,
that does not mean the war is an outlier in terms of how
petro-revoltutionary states tend to behave. [Petro-revolutionary states
like Libya]{.underline} under Muammar Gaddafi [or]{.underline}
[Sudan]{.underline} under Omar al-Bashir tend to [have leaders with
risk-tolerant, aggressive preferences]{.underline}, as demonstrated by
their behavior prior to taking office, which shape their foreign policy
while in office. Both Saddam Hussein and Ruhollah Khomeini seem to fit
that description during the Iran--Iraq War. [Petrostates tend to have
disproportionate military spending during oil booms,]{.underline} which
expands military options such as warfare. Again, military spending in
1970s Iran and Iraq fits this pattern. And petro-revolutionaries often
have oil money to buy off domestic political opponents, who might
otherwise be able to mount opposition to their aggressive policies.
Again, behaviors by Hussein and Khomeini fit that pattern. Thus, [the
Iran--Iraq War does not look an outlier]{.underline} from a theoretical
view. Second, even if we do accept the Iran--Iraq War as a potential
outlier, the question becomes what to do about it. Jang and Smith pursue
one option, but others exist that are at least as valid and arguably
better. For instance, statisticians often prefer a process known as
"winsorization," by which extreme values are limited or capped at
reasonable values near the top end of the distribution. If we winsorize
the Correlates of War's MIDs data, 3 MIDs per state-year is a reasonable
cap because it is the 95th percentile value among state-years with
instigated MIDs. Outside the Iran--Iraq War, there were forty-eight
state-years that had at least 3 MIDs, involving states such as China,
Argentina, or Turkey. So, we can cap any state-year observation at a
value of 3, and rerun the regression. Or, even more pointedly, we could
cap at 3 the observations for Iran and Iraq between 1980 and 1988,
inclusive, but not cap any of the other observations. The results, using
both approaches, are shown in Table 1. In both cases, the coefficient
for petro-revolutionary is positive and above the standard 95%
confidence threshold. In short, we should not treat the Iran--Iraq War
as an outlier, but [even if we do th**e evidence for petro-aggression
remains reasonably strong**.]{.underline} Third, even if we accept the
Iran--Iraq War as an outlier and adopt Jang and Smith's approach to
dealing with it, the coefficient for the key variable
petro-revolutionary is positive and not far from statistical
significance at standard thresholds. In Jang and Smith's statistical
specification with the Iran--Iraq War dummy variable, the coefficient
for petro-revolutionary has a p-value of p = 0.12 (based on their Model
3). Though it is conventional to reject the null hypothesis only if the
p-value is less than 0.05, indicating a 95% confidence level, we should
not affirm the opposite when p = 0.12, indicating only an 88% confidence
level. This known as affirming the null hypothesis, and it is not
warranted just because p is larger than 0.05. Notably, the size of the
coefficient for petro-revolutionary remains large (0.345) in their Model
3, larger than the coefficient for revolutionary government, which is
statistically significant and commonly understood by scholars as
correlated with interstate conflict. That 0.345 coefficient indicates a
31% increase of the expected number of MIDs instigated by such states.
Of course, the standard error for petro-revolutionary is high because
those governments are rare. That creates statistical noise surrounding
the signal. [It is striking that even when we statistically isolate a
major war between two petro-revolutionary states, we still have more
reason to believe]{.underline} (with roughly 88% confidence) [that
petro-revolutionary states are correlated with interstate
conflict]{.underline} than we have reason to believe the null
hypothesis. Petro-aggression also helps us understand the Iran--Iraq War
itself (as a case of two petro-revolutionary states fighting each
other), rather than treating it in a theoretically ad hoc manner. Even
if we treat the war as sui generis, the theory of petro-aggression can
account for the Iran--Iraq War and many other events. The fourth reason
to have confidence in petro-aggression comes from the alternative
measures Jang and Smith use to test its robustness. They spend several
pages in their article criticizing the way I operationalized some of the
variables, describing those measures as "errors." For instance, they
argue that my method of identifying "petrostates" dichotomously (based
on net oil exports) is an error and argue for the preferability of
measuring petrostates based on oil income per capita. They correctly
note that their preferred measure has become common in the literature on
whether oil inhibits democracy, but that does not mean there is only one
true way of identifying petrostates, especially if considering oil's
effect on other dependent variables besides democracy (interstate
conflict, in this case). Jang and Smith's approach suggests that, at
least sometimes, Denmark is more of a petrostate than Nigeria is,
because Denmark has more oil income per capita than Nigeria does. That
is strongly counterintuitive for most people because oil appears to
affect political incentives more in Nigeria than Denmark, relative to
the counterfactual of no oil. By contrast, my measure treats Nigeria as
a petrostate and Denmark as a non-petrostate. Setting aside the
rationale for each measure, it turns out that even when Jang and Smith
use their preferred definition and measure of petrostate, the evidence
again supports petro-aggression. In their Model 9, they expand the scope
of the analysis to include more years than in my analysis, use
alternative specifications of the control variables, and find that the
coefficient for petro-revolutionary is positive and statistically
significant at 95% confidence. In their Model 11, they do the same plus
use their preferred measure of petrostate (based on Michael Ross and
Paasha Mahdavi's oil income per capita data), and again find that the
coefficient for petro-revolutionary is positive and above the 95%
confidence threshold. Only when they add in the control variable for the
Iran--Iraq War does the statistical significance fall below 95% (Models
10 and 12). Thus, [the]{.underline} whole weight of their [critique
stands or falls only based on how we treat the Iran--Iraq War, and
petro-aggression is otherwise quite robust to alternative measures and
data]{.underline}. The fifth reason to have confidence in
petro-aggression comes from the qualitative evidence that I offered in
my 2013 book. Jang and Smith write, "Econometric research could benefit
from reengagement with close-range regional expertise," but they chose
not to engage with the five chapter-length case studies I conducted of
Iraq, Iran, Libya, Venezuela, and Saudi Arabia. The first four of those
cases offer within-country variation with respect to revolutionary
governments; the fifth, Saudi Arabia, serves as a prominent example of
nonrevolutionary petrostates. The book's qualitative analysis
complements the quantitative analysis and offers distinct advantages in
terms of process tracing, congruence testing, and contextual analysis.
[The country-case histories in Petro-Aggression illustrate the causal
mechanisms]{.underline}. For instance, [the cases show that oil revenues
offer petrostate leaders considerable political autonomy from their
population, shielding them from the political backlash if they engage in
military adventurism in foreign policy.]{.underline} Remarkably, no
petro-revolutionary leader who lost a war has been forced out of office
due to domestic political backlash, even though leaders of
non-petrostates who lose wars are frequently punished this way.
Anticipation of those outcomes should induce leaders in non-petrostates
to be cautious about instigating military conflicts, but leaders in
petrostates need not be so cautious. In addition, [there is strong
qualitative evidence that revolutionary leaders tend to be risk
tolerant, which plausibly makes them more likely to engage in interstate
conflict]{.underline}. The sixth and final reason to have confidence in
petro-aggression stems from considering the full range of efforts to
test petro-aggression's empirical robustness. Jang and Smith conclude
that the evidence for petro-aggression is "not robust" and "quite weak,"
phrases which normally suggest the finding is fragile to multiple
alternative specifications. Yet, in my 2010 article and 2013 book on
petro-aggression, I described some of the many robustness checks I
conducted. For instance, I used data from the International Crisis
Behavior dataset as a measure of conflict instead of MIDs. I also used
other measures of the dependent variable, such as the "Side A" indicator
for the conflict initiator. I used four different variants of the
definition of petrostate (for example, continuous measures of oil income
per capita or as fraction of GDP), each of which were substituted for
the dichotomous measure of petrostate, and the regressions retested. I
used logit and rare events logit regressions. The dataset on
revolutionary governments has a secondary variable called ambiguous that
indicate borderline cases or cases where information was missing; that
variable was used for robustness testing. I used dichotomous measures of
democracy instead of continuous ones. I added various potential control
variables, such as: year fixed effects; a lagged dependent variable (to
control for rivalries and ongoing disputes); and the Correlates of War
measure composite indicator of national capability (CINC). The models
were also retested when the sample was restricted to developing
countries only, and when all the independent variables were lagged by
one year, to avoid possible endogeneity. In none of these robustness
tests did any of the results change materially. These tests for
robustness are especially relevant because some of them directly address
the very concerns Jang and Smith raise in their article. They argue that
there are "two key problems with the \[Colgan\] data." The first is:
"The GDP per capita variable drawn from replication ... was lagged in
that study. In the Colgan data, it is not, meaning that every
country-year observation for the indicator is off by one year." Yet, as
I described in the 2010 article, I did lag by one year the GDP per
capita variable (along with the other independent variables). Reasonable
people can differ about whether a one-year lag in the variables is the
right specification, but either way it does not change the result. The
second problem, in Jang and Smith's view, is "the use of outdated
indicators for oil wealth," specifically my dichotomous measure of
petrostate, as opposed to their continuous measure of oil income per
capita. They ignore, however, that I used a continuous measure of oil
income per capita, precisely to address the potential concerns Jang and
Smith describe in their article. Using a continuous oil income measure,
either with the data I used or the Ross and Mahdavi data that Jang and
Smith use in theirs, does not change the basic finding of
petro-aggression. For all these reasons, Jang and Smith's critique seems
unwarranted. [The evidence of petro-aggression has proven
strong.]{.underline} I have set aside Jang and Smith's critique of
Cullen S. Hendrix's work, and I cannot fully engage their claim that oil
has primarily a peace-inducing effect, what they term "Pax Petrolica."
Briefly, I do not think that oil has such a monolithic effect on
interstate politics; instead, oil's net effect on interstate relations
depends significantly on the relevant states' domestic politics. The
main point, however, is that [the basic empirical pattern of
petro-aggression is remarkably robust and stands up well to sustained
critical scrutiny]{.underline}. The interesting question, to my mind, is
not whether the empirical pattern exists, but why. In my original
research, I emphasized the internal properties of petro-revolutionary
states, treating them as monads. In new research, I explore the
possibility that many of these same states became counterhegemonic at
the same time they became revolutionary, and thus they became conflict
prone due to political dynamics at the system and subsystem levels,
rather than at the monadic level. It is quite possible both factors are
at work. Regardless, the evidence as a whole suggests that, for
petrostates with revolutionary governments, the net effect of oil has
been to increase the state's propensity for interstate conflict (not
lower it). In short, **[petro-aggression is a force in world
politics.]{.underline}**

### Petrostates ! - AT Petrostates Inevitable

#### Not inevitable - decarbonization will slash revenues for petrostates

Mike **Coffin** **and** Andrew **Grant**, 2/11/**21**, Carbon Tracker,
"Beyond Petrostates: The burning need to cut oil dependence in the
energy transition,"
https://carbontracker.org/reports/petrostates-energy-transition-report/,
mm

[Fossil fuel-reliant countries could see a drop of 51% in government oil
and gas revenues in a shift to a low-carbon world over]{.underline} the
next two decades. In this report, [we focus on the 40 countries with the
greatest fiscal dependence on oil and gas revenues]{.underline} -- the
petrostates. These are the most oil and gas-reliant countries (as a % of
GDP) and are predominantly in the Middle East, North and West Africa and
South America. [There is a fundamental shift underway as the global
economy begins to decarbonise]{.underline}. Populations that are heavily
reliant on fossil-fuel production face lower government revenues and job
losses as the pace and inevitability of the energy transition increases.
Compared with industry expectations, [petrostates']{.underline}
government [revenues would be \$9 trillion lower over the next two
decades under the low-carbon scenario]{.underline}. The majority of this
decrease is driven by lower prices, rather than lower volumes.

#### Not inevitable -- impending price slumps will reduce oil production in all petrostates

Oliver **Gordon**, 12/3/**23**, Energy Monitor, "Petrostates to lose
\$8trn of fossil-fuel income by 2040,"
https://www.energymonitor.ai/finance/petrostates-to-lose-8trn-of-fossil-fuel-income-by-2040/?cf-view,
mm

COP28 host the United Arab Emirates (UAE) is among [28
petrostates]{.underline} that [risk losing over half their revenues from
fossil fuels by 2040 as the energy transition accelerates]{.underline}
[and climate policies tighten]{.underline}, according to new research
from financial think tank Carbon Tracker. "[Electricity is expanding to
become the basis of our entire energy system]{.underline}, driven by
falling costs of wind, solar and batteries," said Guy Prince, senior oil
and gas analyst at Carbon Tracker and author of the report, in a press
statement. "[This is a profound threat to oil and gas exporting
nations]{.underline}, because falling demand for oil and gas is likely
to lead to a significant fall in future revenues. Governments should
waste no time in reducing their dependence on fossil fuel revenues and
take steps to make their economies more resilient and better equipped
for a low-carbon future." [Demand]{.underline} for oil and gas [is
expected to start declining before the end of the decade]{.underline},
with the subsequent oversupply likely to result in falling prices. This,
the report warns, will leave a large hole in the public finances of the
world's petrostates, even those with the lowest cost of production,
hampering their ability to fund public services and threatening their
stability. The 'Petrostates of Decline' report, released on 1 December,
analyses the potential fossil-fuel revenues of 40 oil and gas dependent
countries between 2023 and 2040 in the scenario of a moderately paced
energy transition that limits global warming to 1.8°C. The nations'
revenues will fall from an expected \$17trn to just \$9trn, the research
found. "In many petrostates, a political settlement has become
established where citizens expect high public sector salaries and low or
zero-income taxes along with a generous welfare state," said Prince.
"Restructuring their economies is likely to require parallel political
reforms to make lawmakers more accountable and give citizens greater
representation." [Governments are adopting increasingly tough climate
policies]{.underline} as natural disasters proliferate and intensify,
and the global carbon budget -- the amount of carbon that can be safely
emitted for the world to stay within a 50% chance of limiting global
warming to 1.5°C -- looks likely to be blown before the decade's end. As
COP28 kicked off in Dubai, nearly 120 countries backed a goal to triple
renewables and double energy efficiency by 2030. Language calling for a
"phasedown/out" of "fossil fuels" and "coal" -- with negotiators still
to decide on the final wording -- was also included in a first draft
text circulated at the COP. Some are pushing for a Fossil Fuel
Non-Proliferation Treaty. [The ever-decreasing cost of renewable
energies]{.underline} and electric vehicles, along with the energy
security concerns spotlighted by the Ukraine War, [is diminishing demand
for oil and gas]{.underline}. According to the IEA's World Energy
Outlook 2023, demand for oil will fall from 100 million barrels a day
(b/d) in 2019 to 92.5 million b/d in 2030 and 54.5 million b/d in 2050
if current national policy pledges are fulfilled. Carbon Tracker warns
that a country like the UAE relies on oil and gas for 40% of government
income, but faces a 60% drop in production revenue. Saudi Arabia, the
world's largest oil exporter, is in a similar situation. In Africa,
Nigeria, Angola, Chad, Congo, Equatorial Guinea and Gabon all rely on
oil and gas for over 60% of their total budgets; and all but Gabon face
a 70% reduction in expected oil and gas revenues. Venezuela is the
country most at risk, with its public finances entirely dependent on oil
and gas revenues, which could be over 80% lower than forecast.
Petrostate governments earn fossil fuel revenues either through
state-owned national oil companies (NOCs) or through taxing oil and gas
production. But [when demand falls, declining prices reduce these
revenues and can render some projects uneconomic.]{.underline} The
highest cost producers will suffer the most, and a moderate transition
is likely to make redundant over 50% of new projects belonging to NOCs
Rosneft (Russia), Ecopetrol (Colombia; this became the tenth government
to join the call for a Fossil Fuel Non-Proliferation Treaty on 2
December) and Pemex (Mexico). However, **[price slumps will impact all
petrostates]{.underline}**. Countries in the Middle East and North
Africa will see 45% less oil and gas revenues under a moderate
transition than a slow one, with nations in Latin America, the Caribbean
and Africa suffering even more.

### Ocean Acidification ! -- Geoengineering Can't Solve

#### Solar geoengineering can't solve ocean acidification

Jessica **McKenzie**, 6/20/**24**, Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists,
"The University of Chicago's new climate initiative,"
https://thebulletin.org/2024/06/the-university-of-chicago-new-climate-initiative/,
mm

Introducing particles like sulfate aerosols into the stratosphere could
create a plethora of new and unpredictable problems. Possible negative
impacts may include changing regional weather patterns---creating or
shifting areas of drought or regions that receive extreme
precipitation---or altering tropospheric chemistry and ocean circulation
patterns. Partially blocking the sun's rays could interfere with normal
plant processes and reduce agricultural yields. Adding sulfate aerosols
to the stratosphere would degrade the ozone layer (thereby increasing
global cancer rates) and increase acid rain. [The]{.underline} potential
[effects]{.underline} [of solar radiation management are]{.underline} so
[large and wide-ranging]{.underline} as to implicate almost every aspect
of life on the planet. [Even in best-case scenarios, it would be only a
partial stopgap]{.underline}. [Solar geoengineering]{.underline}, for
example, [does nothing to ameliorate ocean acidification, which occurs
when the ocean absorbs carbon dioxide from the atmosphere]{.underline}.
[This]{.underline} acidification [threatens ocean life]{.underline} like
oysters, clams, sea urchins, corals, and the calcareous phytoplankton
[that]{.underline} help [make up the foundation of the marine food web
on which much of humanity depends]{.underline}.

#### Solar geoengineering won't decrease emissions -- can't solve ocean acidification 

Michael **Proambo et al., 2024,** National Security Perspective (Johns
Hopkins Applied Physics Laboratory), "Balancing Act: Assessing Risks and
Governance of Climate Intervention,"
https://www.jhuapl.edu/sites/default/files/2024-12/BalancingAct-WEB.pdf,
mm

SRM, also known as sunlight reflection methods or albedo modification,
encompasses techniques that cool the earth by increasing the
reflectivity of solar radiation incident on the planet. Increasing the
reflectivity lowers the amount of solar radiation that is converted to
infrared radiation through absorption and emission. Therefore, less
infrared radiation gets trapped by greenhouse gases, resulting in an
overall cooling effect. Figure 1 depicts this concept and the various
methods within it. [Since SRM methods do not decrease atmospheric carbon
dioxide concentrations, they would not reduce or reverse some physical
consequences of climate change, such as ocean
acidification]{.underline}. Moreover, [if carbon dioxide concentrations
are not simultaneously reduced]{.underline} in other ways, such as CDR
methods, and especially if carbon dioxide concentrations continue to
grow because of emissions, [interruption or termination of SRM methods
could cause significant environmental and societal damage. If SRM were
to be suddenly terminated, the world would warm, perhaps rapidly, based
on the accumulated atmospheric carbon dioxide
concentrations]{.underline}.14 This phenomenon is often referred to as
termination risk, termination shock, or millennial dependence.
Consequently, if SRM were started for climate intervention, it would
need to be perpetually maintained; if the intervention were stopped,
climate change could become much worse.

#### Geoengineering can't solve ocean acidification -- only GHG reductions can

Phil **McKenna**, 9/5/**15**, Inside Climate News, "Geoengineering will
not save ocean life from acidification, research says,"
https://insideclimatenews.org/news/05082015/geoengineering-will-not-save-ocean-acidification-global-warming/,
mm

[Waiting to tackle ocean acidification]{.underline} caused by climate
change [through]{.underline} yet-to-be developed
[geoengineering]{.underline} schemes [will be too little too late to
prevent mass extinction of ocean life]{.underline}, a new study
concludes. [Cutting carbon emissions is the only way for oceans to
recover]{.underline} from the devastating effects of climate change,
according to the new research published in Nature Climate Change. While
using deliberate, large-scale manipulation of earth processes to combat
global warming has its proponents, intervening in the climate through
potentially dangerous geoengineering technologies is unproven. And even
if carbon dioxide could be removed from the atmosphere, ocean
acidification already spurred by CO2 will persist for centuries. This
could cause mass extinctions of marine species, researchers concluded.
["Once the ocean is severely affected by high carbon dioxide, it is
virtually impossible to undo these alterations on a human-generation
timescale]{.underline}," said Sabine Mathesius of the Potsdam Institute
for Climate Impact Research in Potsdam, Germany. Mathesius and
colleagues used computer modeling to simulate the effects of CO2 removal
on ocean acidification under a high emissions, business-as-usual
scenario. Acidification, or a decrease in the water's pH level, occurs
when CO2 is absorbed by seawater and reacts by producing acid. Increased
ocean acidity harms a wide range of organisms including coral, mussels,
clams, sea urchins, barnacles and microscopic plankton that use calcium
carbonate, a mineral that dissolves with increased ocean acidity, to
build their shells and other structures. Damage to coral reefs is one
well-known example of the effects of increased ocean acidity, but such
acidification affects all organisms that rely on calcium carbonate
including coccolithophores, phytoplankton found in large numbers
throughout the ocean. "They are really at the base of a lot of food
chains," said Steven Davis, an earth systems scientist at the University
of California, Irvine. "[If oceans are acidified those things are going
to suffer and it could destabilize whole webs of the food
chain]{.underline}." The group looked at the effect of removing CO2 from
the atmosphere at two rates. The first rate, 5 gigatons of carbon per
year, equals half of today's annual CO2 emissions. They then modeled a
more aggressive rate of 25 gigatons of carbon per year, or 2.5 times
current annual emissions. Even the higher removal rate, which the
authors describe as "probably infeasible," would be unable to return the
oceans to preindustrial conditions by 2700, which was the end of the
simulation period. [The study also looked at whether a high CO2 emission
rate, followed by intensive carbon removal, would have the same effect
on the oceans as a continuously low rate of carbon emissions achieved
through immediate reductions]{.underline}. [The simulations
showed]{.underline} near-surface ocean waters would respond quickly to a
geoengineered removal of CO2 from the atmosphere, but [the deep ocean
would take several centuries or more to approach the pH levels of a
continuous low-emissions scenario]{.underline}. ["There really is no
replacement for]{.underline} drastically [cutting]{.underline} our
[emissions]{.underline} now," said Davis. "Swallowing a spider to catch
a fly won't work." Davis said he doubted whether removing carbon dioxide
from the atmosphere on such a large scale is even achievable. One
controversial technique to pull carbon from the atmosphere is to grow
biomass---crops, plant or tree matter, which suck up CO2 as they
grow---burn it to generate electricity, and then capture and bury all of
its CO2 emissions deep underground. The Intergovernmental Panel on
Climate Change has endorsed the carbon-sucking technique given the
urgent need to shrink the earth's carbon footprint, with a caveat that
considerable scientific uncertainty remains about its risks and costs.
To remove large amount of carbon from the atmosphere, it would likely
take industrial methods that use chemicals to capture carbon dioxide
directly from the air. That approach is currently being tested in small
scale pilots by companies like Carbon Engineering and Global Thermostat
but the cost would be prohibitively expensive, Davis said. "We are
talking about vastly larger dollars than anyone has suggested it would
cost to switch over to renewables today," Davis said. "You are getting
towards the GDP of large economies." Graciela Chichilnisky, an economist
at Columbia University and the chief executive of Global Thermostat,
said her company is developing a CO2 removal system that will
significantly reduce costs by harnessing waste heat from coal-fired
power plants to power their operations. Chichilnisky said atmospheric
removal of C02 must be developed along with energy like solar power and
other renewables. She cited a 2013 report by the Intergovernmental Panel
on Climate Change that states "a large fraction of anthropogenic climate
change resulting from CO2 emissions is irreversible on a multi-century
to millennial time scale, except in the case of a large net removal of
CO2 from the atmosphere over a sustained period." "You need to do \[CO2
removal and develop low-emissions energy\] but the one you need to do
right now is the removal of CO2 in massive quantities from the air,"
Chichilnisky said. [Some]{.underline}, however, [fear that even talk of
the potential to remove carbon dioxide from the atmosphere]{.underline}
at a later date [will make countries more likely to put off emissions
reductions]{.underline}. [The resulting increase in ocean acidification
from such a delay **could cause irreversible catastrophic
changes**]{.underline}, Davis said. A 2008 study published in the Annals
of the New York Academy of Sciences notes that "past mass extinction
events have been linked to ocean acidification, and the current rate of
change in seawater chemistry is unprecedented." Davis says if emissions
continue at current levels the effect it will have on oceans could
spread beyond the marine environment. "The oceans are this real buffer
to the earth's systems," Davis said. "We are forcing it slowly but
surely and the problem is once we get it moving in a direction it is
really hard to reverse that."

#### Solar geoengineering will intensify ocean acidification -- cooling the ocean surface without reducing C02 intensifies the acidification of the deep oceans -- that collapses marine ecosystems

Kimberly **Samuels-Crow**, 11/26/**24**, SRM 260, "SRM Cannot Address
Ocean Acidification,"
https://srm360.org/article/srm-cannot-address-ocean-acidification/, mm

CO2 emissions, the main driver of human-caused climate change, have also
acidified the ocean since the start of the industrial revolution. Ocean
acidification is the result of chemical reactions between CO2 in the air
and water that make key shell-building compounds less available for
marine organisms, weakening their skeletons and shells. This would
threaten the foundation of marine food webs, impacting commercial and
indigenous fisheries,1 the livelihoods of 300 million people, and a
major source of protein for more than half of the world's population.2
Today, the ocean has an average pH3 of around 8.05. While seawater has a
pH above 7 and is therefore alkaline, it is more acidic than it has been
in the past 2 million years and is at least 25% more acidic now than
before the industrial revolution. [Sunlight reflection
methods]{.underline} (SRM) like stratospheric aerosol injection (SAI)
[cannot directly address the ocean acidification problem]{.underline}.
Instead [SRM could enhance the ability of some ecosystems on land to
retain carbon, decreasing CO2 in the atmosphere]{.underline}.4 [However,
CO2 dissolves more readily in colder water. This enhanced CO2 solubility
could cancel out any benefit of SRM on the carbon cycle and **may lead
to increased acidity** at greater ocean depths]{.underline}.5 The ocean
absorbs CO2 at its surface, taking in about 25% of human-caused CO2
emissions per year.6 The ocean's capacity to absorb CO2 makes it the
second largest carbon reservoir after the solid earth, partially
mitigating the warming effects of fossil fuel combustion.7 However, the
accumulation of CO2 in the ocean since the start of the industrial
revolution has affected ocean water chemistry and marine life. When
atmospheric CO2 dissolves in water, it reacts to form carbonic acid
(H2CO3) -- the substance that gives sparkling water its sharp or acidic
taste. As CO2 emissions increase, carbonic acid in the ocean also
increases, making the ocean more acidic.8 Once CO2 reacts with water,
forming carbonic acid, a series of chemical reactions takes place. These
chemical reactions ultimately decrease the relative abundance of
carbonate ions (CO32−), which shell-forming organisms use to build their
shells.9 Models predict ocean acidity will continue to increase. By the
end of the 21st century, models predict pH will decrease by around 0.01
if emissions are significantly cut (scenario SSP1-1.9) or around 0.39 in
an extreme high-emissions scenario (scenario SSP5-8.5).10 [Increasingly
acidic water could lead to extensive die-offs of]{.underline} coral
reefs and exacerbate harm to other [marine ecosystems]{.underline}.7
Ocean acidification, through its effects on shell-forming organisms,
ultimately impacts the people and industries that rely on the ocean.
Increased ocean acidification makes it more difficult for organisms,
including shellfish and tiny organisms that form the basis of the marine
food web, to build and maintain shells.11 This causes shells to become
thinner or dissolve.9 Impacts on shell-builders affect other animals
that rely on them for food, including humans. Globally, economic losses
in the shellfish industry alone are estimated to be \$6 billion to \$100
billion per year by 2100.1 Loss of shellfish will also have major
impacts on indigenous and rural groups​​.1 Ocean acidification has
additional impacts on the concentrations of harmful pollutants that fish
accumulate, which ultimately pose threats to human health. For example,
fish in acidified waters accumulate more mercury, aluminium, iron, zinc,
copper, and lead in their tissues, posing a poisoning hazard to people
who rely on fish for protein.2 Coral reefs are also at risk from warming
and ocean acidification. Around the world, an estimated 500 million
people rely on coral reefs for food, coastal protection, and income.1
Though it is difficult to assign a cost to these valuable "ecosystem
services", estimates range from \$29.8 billion to \$376 billion per
year.1 The ocean's capacity to store carbon is changing as the climate
changes. Increased acidification, warmer ocean temperatures, and changes
in wind and storm patterns alter the amount of CO2 the ocean can absorb
and store.12,13 [Without interventions, the ocean's ability to store
carbon is likely to weaken as the climate continues to
change]{.underline}.14 [This would lead to more CO2 in the atmosphere
and increased warming]{.underline}. Suggested sunlight reflection
methods (SRM) like stratospheric aerosol injection (SAI) are not
expected to have a direct impact on ocean acidification but could have
indirect effects through the global carbon cycle, i.e., the flow of
carbon atoms between Earth's oceans, atmosphere, and land.5 SRM could
decrease temperatures, enhancing the ability of non-marine carbon sinks
to retain carbon or remove CO2 from the air. For example, [by cooling
the Arctic, SAI could slow permafrost melting and the associated carbon
loss to the atmosphere]{.underline}.4 Further, SAI, by scattering light,
could enhance the ability of plants to take CO2 from the atmosphere.5
SRM could change the distribution of acidity in the ocean relative to a
climate without SRM deployment. [Without SRM, ocean currents that
transport CO2 to the deep ocean are expected to weaken. With SRM, these
currents are expected to stay strong]{.underline}.15 [Therefore, the net
effect of SRM in a high-CO2 world would likely be little change in
acidity at the ocean surface **but increased acidity at greater
depths**]{.underline}.5 [To tackle rising ocean acidification, CO2
emissions would need to be eliminated]{.underline}, and to reverse it
would require large-scale deployment of carbon dioxide removal methods
to safely store CO2 away from the atmosphere and ocean.

## Offcase Args

### DA -- Turns Case -- Termination Shock

#### The DA turns case -- the impact ensures a system failure, triggering a termination shock and runaway warming

Robert **Olson**, November **2011**, Wilson Center, "Geoengineering for
decision makers,"
https://www.wilsoncenter.org/sites/default/files/media/documents/publication/Geoengineering_for_Decision_Makers_0.pdf,
mm

This concern stems directly from the possibility that of the prospect of
[successful]{.underline} SRM [geoengineering would make emissions
reductions seem less urgent and take the heat off
politicians]{.underline}. Then, as the editors of Scientific American
recently wrote, [carbon dioxide would continue to build up in the
atmosphere]{.underline}, "breaching the level of 450 parts per million
by volume (ppmv) that many climatologists now recommend as the upper
limit, then passing the 550 ppmv mark that is the goal of many current
policy initiatives, and eventually reaching ... a level not seen on
Earth since the days of the dinosaurs." [The danger then becomes that if
the geoengineering effort should ever falter, "a century's worth of
warming would hit us]{.underline}."35 [It would hit with a speed
unprecedented in the Earth's history, making adaptation nearly
impossible]{.underline} for large numbers of plant and animal species,
and perhaps for human society Unchecked carbon dioxide emissions would
build up in the ocean as well as the atmosphere. Carbon dioxide has a
lifetime in the atmosphere of about a hundred years, but large amounts
of CO2 stored in the ocean would outgas for a much longer time, keeping
the atmosphere laden with CO2 . This means that any
[geoengineering]{.underline} initiatives that allow such build-ups to
occur [would need to have]{.underline} internationally sanctioned
[control that is ultra-reliable and proof against mechanical failures,
human error, economic depressions and funding failures, wars, terrorism,
natural disasters and simple apathy across many centuries]{.underline}.
It may be, therefore, that the social and institutional requirements for
some forms of geoengineering are much more daunting than the engineering
requirements.36

#### A nuclear war would disrupt geoengineering -- triggers termination shock and turns the case

Scott **Barrett**, Summer **2014**, Review of Environmental Economics
and Policy, 8(2), "Solar Geoengineering's Brave New World: Thoughts on
the Governance of an Unprecedented Technology,"
https://www.journals.uchicago.edu/doi/10.1093/reep/reu011, mm

Ironically, [one argument against]{.underline} doing [geoengineering is
that we might someday stop doing it]{.underline} (Matthews and Caldeira
2007). [This]{.underline} so-called [termination problem is based on the
fear that we might stop]{.underline} geoengineering
[suddenly]{.underline}, long after we started using it, [causing
temperatures to rise much more rapidly than if we had never
geoengineered in the first place]{.underline} (Goes, Tuana, and Keller
2011). Of course, the incentives to terminate geoengineering abruptly
would be very low for precisely this reason. It seems more likely that
if geoengineering produced unexpected problems long after we started
using it, countries would reduce its use gradually while at the same
time taking other actions, such as increasing investments in adaptation
and efforts to reduce atmospheric concentrations of CO2.12 Of course,
[there is]{.underline} also [the possibility that geoengineering would
be terminated abruptly]{.underline} (and unavoidably) [because of an
unrelated catastrophe, such as nuclear war]{.underline} or an asteroid
strike. But because geoengineering could be deployed unilaterally by a
great number of countries, problems in one country or region would not
prevent the redeployment of geoengineering somewhere else. [Only a truly
global catastrophe could cause geoengineering to fail
permanently,]{.underline} and in such a case the global community would
likely have more serious problems to address than the unmanaged
termination of geoengineering.

### Environmental Management K -- Link

#### Geoengineering is a techno-quick fix attempt to manage the unmanageable -- controlling with the atmosphere will divorce us from the natural world -- the technological intervention will eventually fail which turns the Aff

Jason **Mark**, **2009**, Earth Island Journal, "Hacking the Sky,"
https://www.earthisland.org/journal/index.php/magazine/entry/hacking_the_sky/##,
mm

[The idea of dimming the sun carries a number of problems]{.underline}.
First, take the ethical conundrum of unequal benefits. What if world
leaders decided to deploy the sulfur option and, as one climate model
has suggested, an engineered cooling led to a decrease in monsoon rains
over Asia? In such a scenario, geo-engineering could benefit some 5
billion people, while putting another 2 billion people in danger of
drought and famine. The risk of unequal benefits connects to a second
difficult question: Who would control such powerful technology? Few
people would want the US (or Chinese) military to run the weather.
Corporate control would have its own drawbacks. As Robock put it to me:
"Would you trust the ExxonMobil geo-engineering unit?" Leaving
management of a makeshift sky to the lowest bidder seems imprudent, to
say the least. Thernstrom says one of the virtues of geo-engineering is
precisely this centralized control. While unilateral emissions
reductions are pointless, unilateral geo-engineering could work. Any
industrial power could likely do it on its own -- which means you don't
need collective action to cool the planet; you just need countries not
to object. But [even if the major powers agreed to cool the globe,
reaching consensus on how exactly wouldn't be simple]{.underline}. "How
do we even decide what the temperature of the planet will be?" Robock
wonders. "Whose hand will be on the thermostat? What if Russia and
Canada decide they want it warmer and India wants it cooler? How do you
decide those things?" Imagine that the United Nations took control of
the planetary thermostat. That would prevent any country from having a
monopoly over geo-engineering or, worse, having several countries deploy
geo-engineering at cross-purposes. But UN oversight would still involve
geo-politics. It's been close to impossible to get the major polluters
to agree to emissions reductions. Finding cooperation on something as
powerful as geo-engineering would be at least as complicated. That's a
concern of James Lovelock, founder of the Gaia theory. Lovelock's new
book, The Vanishing Face of Gaia, warns that climate change will wreck
civilization. Still, he doesn't think that geo-engineering provides a
way out. "If we can't predict what's happening now, how can we predict
what's happening in 50 years with some kind of artificial mechanism?" he
said to me in a conversation this summer. "It's just moonshine. I think
that [if we ever take on the task of trying to manage the planet
completely -- if we succeed with geo-engineering and we have to run the
planet ourselves]{.underline}, doing what the system now does for free
-- [that we will be on a course for extinction]{.underline}. [Because we
can never manage it. We haven't learned to live with ourselves
yet]{.underline}." As Lovelock points out, [the political and ethical
issues are compounded by an epistemological predicament: No one knows
how the planet would react]{.underline}. [Geo-engineering is unlike any
experiment in history]{.underline} in that the subject is the entire
globe. [On a closed system floating in space, there is no laboratory to
test ideas]{.underline}. "I think geo-engineering is less an ethical
question than a methodological question," Martin Bunzl, a philosopher
who works closely with Robock, said to me. "Could you answer the risk
analysis with enough assurance to deploy at a large scale? The burden of
proof is on the proponents to tell us we know enough about how the
atmosphere works." Take the sulfur aerosol proposal. Would stratospheric
injection of SO2 rip a hole in the ozone layer? Would it decrease the
amount of energy that solar panels capture or, far more troublesome,
affect how plants grow? What if it caused a massive drought in Africa?
These are the known unknowns. More worrisome are the unknown unknowns --
the consequences we can't even imagine. "The difference with large-scale
geo-engineering is that you can't actually proceed in the normal way
that science proceeds: lab to field tests to increased levels of
deployment," Bunzl says. "Because you don't have a model that models the
whole world system well enough. You can only deploy the whole thing. Or
you are trying to make an inference from a small-scale deployment? What
will the consequences be at full strength?" Without a laboratory, any
test to see how the atmosphere would react is already a manipulation of
the atmosphere. "The problem with sulfur insertion is that you can't get
results until you get to a certain strength, and you can't do it without
involving the whole atmosphere," Bunzl says. Or, put another way: The
only way to investigate the results of tinkering with the sky is to
tinker with the sky. The experiment is itself a fait accompli. The
epistemological checkmate means that the very term "geo-engineering" is
flawed. [Fixing the climate isn't like repairing a bridge or building a
skyscraper.]{.underline} The planet is neither an engine nor, in the
metaphor used at the beginning of this essay, a supercomputer. [It's an
enormous living system, intricate beyond the scale of human
understanding]{.underline}, our impressive discoveries notwithstanding.
A machine has certain parts that work in expected ways: Even when
moving, an engine is static. That's why it's reliable. [Earth
is]{.underline} different: It is, by nature, [ceaselessly
dynamic]{.underline}. So [we can't be certain about the outcome of a
given input.]{.underline} [Despite all our fancy computer modeling, we
will never know for sure how the atmosphere will respond to
manipulation]{.underline}. More than an endeavor of science,
geo-engineering would be an act of faith. Beyond the political and
scientific questions lies a much larger moral, even spiritual, problem:
Do humans have the right to undertake such a monumental task? The
geo-engineering debate proves once again that while our technological
society is adept at exploring the how, we are less practiced in pursuing
why and whether. As [geo-engineering]{.underline} proponents
acknowledge, schemes like sulfur aerosol [address only the symptoms, not
the source, of global climate change]{.underline}. [That fact betrays
our society's bias for the techno-fix,]{.underline} the seemingly easy
way out. Seemingly -- because [geo-engineering]{.underline} is the most
complicated strategy we could pursue. It [takes a problem, simplifies
its cause, and then exaggerates its solution]{.underline}. It's like a
Rube Goldberg machine, employing eight or nine steps when one or two
would do. Instead of pursuing the elegant solutions -- trading in our
cars for buses, turning off the coal and turning on the wind -- we are
going to build a contraption to make the clouds shinier. Bill Becker,
head of the Presidential Climate Action Project, summed up this thinking
in an essay earlier this year: "Geo-engineering is rooted in the idea
that although we're too stupid to do the simple things that would slow
climate change, we're smart enough to do the improbable things." Indeed,
geo-engineering involves a surfeit of technological imagination and a
poverty of political imagination, an imbalance that's ingrained in the
notion that if we can do something we should do it. We prefer the overly
complicated solutions because they flatter us, confirming our power and
intelligence. This makes geo-engineering -- the ambivalence of its
promoters notwithstanding -- human hubris compounded. It's like doubling
down on self-regard. [Geo-engineering is a bet that we can save
civilization by divorcing our species from the rest of the
globe.]{.underline} [The payoff is the idea that in "fixing" the planet,
we can absolve ourselves of having ruined Earth. The risk is that if we
turn the atmosphere into]{.underline} what Dale Jamieson, director of
environmental studies at NYU, calls [a "human artifact," we will lose
our connection to much of what is best in life]{.underline}. In taking
possession of the sky, we will become ungrounded. [The psychological
ramifications of geo-engineering shouldn't be
underestimated]{.underline}. It's exactly what Bill McKibben worried
about 20 years ago in his seminal book on global warming, The End of
Nature, when he warned of "the imposition of our artificial world in
place of the broken natural one. ... How can there be a mystique of the
rain now that every drop ... bears the permanent stamp of man? Having
lost its separateness, it loses its special power. Instead of being a
category like God -- something beyond our control -- it is now a
category like the defense budget or the minimum wage, a problem we must
work out. This in itself changes its meaning completely, and changes our
reaction to it." [Tinker with the heavens, and our relationship to the
rest of the world suffers. We will sever our bonds to the other natural
systems]{.underline} -- rivers, forests, oceans -- [on which we
depend]{.underline}. We will have made a decision that we can live
without those things. [Once we take responsibility for managing the
planet's curtains, our position in this place changes]{.underline}. [We
will be in charge in a way we never have been before, knowing that
if]{.underline} for any reason we [were to cease overseeing the
sunlight]{.underline}, [global temperatures would shoot upward again,
spelling disaster.]{.underline} [The new role will force upon us an
existential anxiety]{.underline}. Because as soon as we are in control
of the weather, we will always be fearful of letting our grip slip from
the string that keeps the planet in a semblance of balance. [Such
ownership]{.underline} of Earth would be a new step in human evolution.
It [would turn us into a bubble species, living inside a protective dome
of our own making. If that comes to pass, we will cease to view the
world as a comfort. It will have become,]{.underline} instead, [a
threat.]{.underline}

The second condition to the US deployment of MCB \[marine cloud
brightening\] is a sufficient level of domestic support for the

democracies. Nevertheless, empirical studies reveal that public opinion
strongly influences policy, especially where the salience of an issue is
high, and politicians are often responsive to public demands on climate
change. [In the case of MCB, the unprecedented nature of the deployment,
together with its significant financial cost, is likely to heighten the
salience of the public debate]{.underline}. Further, because MCB would
